Then cut the bridge between the two ligaments; you can do this at that hour or later, and if you wish, you can cauterize it to the bone with a blade until its edges split. The position of this procedure will be as we have said or better.
If bleeding occurs during the procedure, quickly cut it off either by cauterization or by filling the area with vinegar and pressing your hand until the bleeding stops. If you do not have anything for that purpose, place your finger on it to clot the blood.
Then pour cold water on the area to reduce swelling and press it as needed. This is easier than cauterizing the vessels; just cauterize the artery with this blade after applying ointment to both sides.
And it descends very strongly until it reaches the bone, and the nerve is cut in two places so that what is between its ends does not adhere. This is the appearance of the syringe.
The blades will resemble the small scissors, except that they are less sharp than the blade by a great deal because if they were as sharp as the blade, the cold would enter them faster and the flesh would not cut quickly. Therefore, there should be some dullness in them so that the fire's heat can stay in them.
The flesh is cut quickly, and this operation is the best of all operations, the least painful, and the easiest. After that, there should be between the blades a degree of dullness equal to the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of constant inflammation of the lacrimal ducts.
If the tears are constant and their inflammation is from the vessels on the outside of the skull and no benefit is derived from medical treatment with drugs, and you see the face of the patient has become red. And he feels in his temples a pulsation like the pulsation of an ant, and his eyes are swollen, wet, their margins having been eaten away by tears and constricted by the sharpness of the tears. Therefore, the early physicians were compelled to resort to this treatment, which they call "treatment with the sword," which is to order the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then make three parallel incisions on the forehead, each about two fingerbreadths long. The first incision is in the middle of the forehead, aligned with the length of the nose, the second is slightly below the muscle on the cheek, and the third is on the opposite side.
And be careful not to cut the veins that are in the temples, far from where the jaws meet. The distance between each incision should be about three fingerbreadths. Make sure you have plenty of prepared cloth or clean rags to soak up the blood.
Then insert the pointed, sharp probe as shown in this figure. From the incision next to the cheek to the middle incision, use it to scrape all the skin between them along with the periosteum on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision and then remove this probe. Then also insert into the first incision another instrument called a sharp knife, which is sharp on one side and blunt on the other, as shown in this figure:
Then turn its sharp side upward, toward the meat attached to the skin, and its blunt side toward the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision and cut with it all the veins that descend from the head to the eyes, but without cutting into the outer layer of the skin.
Then do the same in the middle part towards the other end, and after a reasonable amount of blood has drained, compress the areas with solidified blood clots. Place a piece of cotton wool on each opening and cover it with a pad that has been soaked in wine or olive oil.
Unless there is severe inflammation, on the third day uncover the bandage and use abundant warm water to wash it. Then treat it with a salve of rose oil and everything else that wounds are treated with until recovery, God willing.
Chapter Five.
Treatment of tears and discharge from the eyes due to a condition inside the head.
When someone experiences frequent, painful, and persistent discharge from the eyes, with the eyes appearing as small, weak, red, and swollen, and their lashes fall out. And there is deep, severe pain in the head and persistent headache, know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep vessels. The best treatment for this is to shave the patient's forehead and then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is deep, severe pain in the head and persistent headache, know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep vessels. The best treatment for this is to shave the patient's forehead and then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. And raise slightly to the side, and begin the incision from the left cheek to the right cheek, and it will be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the moving tendons of the cheeks when chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is wiped with a sponge.
Separate the incision between the two cheeks with a thick piece of cotton or linen, then bind it from above with bandages and soak the bandages in water and oil to prevent burning heat, and when you remove them and see that the hot inflammation has passed, it is necessary to feel the bone until meat growth begins in it.
Then treat it with dry dressing that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colocynth, preparing a poultice from them and applying it to the meat growth in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of ear discharge.
Whatever discharges from the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone like iron and glass, or a plant seed like a grain or a nut and the like, or a fluid thing such as water and vinegar and the like.
If it is an animal, then when a pebble or something like a pebble falls into the ear and does not come out of the ear, then turn the sun to the ear. If you see the pebble, put some fat from a sheep or a goat on it, then try to remove it by moving the head or blowing with a pipe. Block the openings at the onset of the sneezing, after placing a ring of leather or wool around the ear and tilting the ear upwards. This treatment often causes it to come out. If it does not come out, try to remove it with gentle blowing as shown in this figure.
If it does not come out with blowing, try to remove it with a fine, dark probe of little thickness. If it still does not come out, then make a tube from copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal gently.
Seal around the tube with wax mixed with oil so that the air has no path except through the tube, then draw it out with a strong breath. This method often causes it to come out. If it does not come out with what we have described, then take some glue from the ants or processed glue, which is used to catch birds. Place it at the end of the tube.
After covering it with a firm cotton cloth, insert it into the ear hole using tweezers after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out with everything we described, rush to make an incision before the inflammation becomes severe or pus forms.
The incision method is to first weaken the patient in the neck area, then extract blood from him according to his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands and turn his ear upwards. Make a small incision at the base of the ear where it is lowest.
The incision should be crescent-shaped, reaching the bone, then remove it with the tools available to you. Then quickly close the incision from where you are and treat it until it heals.
If what fell into the ear is one of the seeds that swell and grow, try to remove it as mentioned before. If it does not come out, then take a very thin, delicate wire like this.
Try to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear with it, and you only do this if you are sure that the seed has softened with the steam of the ear until it becomes small, many pieces. Then remove it with a black thread or a delicate hook or tweezers.
It is easy to expel it, and as for the water inside the ear, it is appropriate for the patient to use a syringe first, after having filled his ear with some cotton wool while lying on that ear which contains the water, so it will come out.
Alternatively, many thin and flat pebbles should be taken along the length of the fingers. These should then be warmed slightly over a fire. The patient should insert one into his earhole and place his foot on that side, then strike with another stone on the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with stone after stone until all the water comes out. The water may also come out by taking a feather or a reed, inserting one end into the ear and burning the other end in the fire until it is almost burnt, then another reed should be used again.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out, or use a syringe as mentioned before with stones. As for expelling an animal inside, if it is small like a fly, treat it as mentioned in the division.
If its body is large and visible, try to remove it with forceps and tweezers; this is easier than any other thing that grows in the ear. As for removing the worms born in the ear, if you treated it with what we mentioned in the chapter on ear infections and it did not heal,
You should look at the ear in the sunlight; if you see anything from the worms, remove it with forceps or fine tweezers. If nothing appears to you from it, then take this tube and shape it like this.
Make its lower part narrow and the upper part wide; then insert its thin end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate. Then press it with strong pressure, repeating this several times until all the worms come out. If they do not come out, smear wax around the tube as I mentioned to you in the case of the stone.
If they do not come out with what we mentioned, use the ointments that I have described, which the ancients used to kill worms. You will find this in the chapter on ointments. With this device, you can pour the medications and ointments into the ear as shown here:
You see it is made of silver or narrow copper tube, the bottom being perforated with a small hole, and the top wide open. If you wish, let the shield within the tube be of thick copper, and if you prefer, take a reed and wrap its end tightly with tight cotton wool.
Then take the oil or juice, or whatever medication you want from these, and pour it into the tube while it is in the ear; then insert the reed with the cotton into the opening and press it gently with your hand until the liquid runs into the ear canal and settles therein.
The patient should lie down, and whatever is poured into the ear should be warmed a little over fire; but take care that what is poured in is not very cold or very hot, for the ear does not tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On treating excessive obstruction of the ear.
Some children come out of their mothers' wombs with their ears unperforated.
In some cases, people may also experience obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or swollen flesh. This obstruction can be deep within the ear canal, invisible to the naked eye.
And it may protrude slightly outside the aperture and be visible, in which case it is usually difficult to extract. If it is visible, it should be placed in the sun. And look into it, for if you see the obstruction visible, open it with a fine probe, and this is its form:.
Its tip should have a slight width, limited to its upper part, and the rest of the probe should be smooth on both sides so as not to hurt the ear. If the obstruction is from flesh that has grown in the passage of the ear, grasp it with a fine forceps and cut it off with great care until all the flesh is removed.
But if the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a fine, smooth spatula and heat it gently in the fire. Then insert it into the passage of the ear. If you feel the obstruction along with what the patient feels as heaviness in the hearing, then crush it with forceps.
And be careful not to cut the nerve of the ear, then insert into the ear a bandage according to the size of the aperture, which has been soaked in Egyptian honey. Do this for several days until you are sure that the wound is healing, or take a bandage and soak it in water and sprinkle on it powdered gum.
Use it, for if you see the ear has been cauterized and a hot worm is in it, it is necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another that has been dipped in an oil made from roses until the hot worm subsides, then treat it until it heals.
If there is a bloody discharge, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, then follow the rest of the treatment mentioned in the section on catarrh.
Chapter Eight
In treating polyps that appear in the nose.
These polyps that appear in the nasal passages may be moist or dry; it is necessary to grasp them with tweezers or a hook and cut them off at their roots, then apply powdered lime to the place.
If there is a bloody discharge from it, cauterize it with a fine glass cupping glass; this is the best treatment after cutting it, and if any part of its root remains, it will often return. But if you burn it with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter Nine
In treating excessive cold in the nasal passages of the nose.
Many things appear in the armpits resembling coldness in their severity and rigidity.
Therefore, it is called "cold" and is the accumulation of thick moisture in the upper armpit and the lower armpit. The action on them is to observe; if the coldness appears prominent on the surface of the armpit, moving towards all sides, then its treatment is easy: apply a broad incision over it.
Then scrape it from all directions with a knife until it is removed, and suture it with silk and cut off the stitches. If you cannot prepare to cut it except after the armpit has healed from cutting, this will not harm the patient at all. If the incision is large, gather it together with sutures and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, do not worry about it, for the pus will force it out and soften it. If the coldness leans towards the inner surface of the armpit, turn the armpit inside out and suture the coldness with silk without needing an incision, and treat it from all directions.
If the armpit heals from the incision, this will not harm in any way. Then wash the area after cutting out the coldness with salt water and treat the place with what softens until the patient recovers.
The Tenth Chapter
On treating the sty that appears in the corner of the eye.
The sty is a pustule that forms in the upper layers of the eye's corner, and this usually happens to children. It weighs down their eyes and causes tears in the evenings, and they cannot look at the sunlight. Because tears speed up the process.
Therefore, you will see them constantly leaning on their faces, and they sleep this way. Their eyes are under the covers, wet from blowing or sucking on the sty, which is now evident to the eye. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the sty between them. The method of treatment is for the patient to place his head on a stone.
Then take a piece of cloth and make a thread from it, and from that thread make a ring the size that fits around the sty from all sides. Place it on top and press with your fingers from all sides to gather the sty in the center of the ring.
Then make a hole in the center of that wetness with a hot needle, making the hole wide and not larger than the hole in a lancet. As for depth, it is necessary to pierce all the skin until you reach the sty.
In most cases, the stylet protrudes from the incision like a white piece of fat. You should remove it using tweezers that you have wrapped with thread between your fingers and then move your hand to the right and left until it is released. Be careful not to increase the incision so that the stylet does not fall into the eye and hurt it.
If the stylet does not appear at first, you should widen the incision slightly until the stylet becomes visible. Then remove it as previously mentioned, soak the tweezers in vinegar and water, place them on the wound, and press with a bandage. Some people crush salt over it.
He places it in the depth of the incision so that any remaining moisture dissolves, then treats it until it heals. If a hot abscess occurs at the site, he treats it with cool compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
In Cases of Burning the Eye.
If an excessive amount of hair grows in the corner of the eye, below the natural eyelashes and beyond its limit, it can harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, inflammation of the corners, whitening, and thickening to the point that it becomes a cause of blindness. ︎
Cauterizing the eye is done in four ways: either by cautery with fire or with a sharp drug as mentioned in the chapter on cautery, or it may be by excision and suturing or by probing according to what I remember.
You should place the patient's head on your lap, then turn the eyelid inside out with your left hand. If it doesn’t turn completely, insert a needle into it from below the eyelid, and pass the thread through the needle from above. This should be close to the same length as the hair, and then pull the thread upwards through the eyelid.
Then turn it inside out again with the pincers, and make an incision in the depths of the eyelid without cutting too much hair, from the larger muscle to the smaller one. Then draw the thread tightly and place a small piece of cotton or linen under the eyelid. Finally, close the eyelid with a bandage.
It should be shaped like a piece of bread, except that the shape should correspond to how much you want to lift the eyelid because this varies among people. Some may need to have part of the eyelid cut off, depending on how much the eyelid has drooped.
...and among them, those who need to have the least of all cut off according to the extent of their desire, then cauterize with the cautery on the two lines that I have taught, beginning from the greater toe to the smaller one, and let there be one cautery near the natural hair with the likeness of the thickness of a strand.
Then insert the reed into one of the corners of the skin, then shave all of it, then join the lips together with a needle and fine woolen thread, and wipe away the blood and stick what remains of the thread to the temples with some adhesive substances.
Whether you choose to do that or not, it does not matter. Leave the sutures and the threads for about three or four days, then treat it. If you wish, leave the wound without suturing it and treat it with something that dries and tightens, because the hair will stand up when the wound is closed and sutured, and stitching is better.
This method of cauterization, which was mentioned before, has the advantage that it depends on the patient and is a good procedure with no risk. There is another method in cauterization, which is to shape the hair like the shape of an almond leaf, as we have described. Then raise the cheek with three retractors that are either separated or grouped as shown in this image:
Then, cut off the excess cheek with small scissors as shown in this image, making cuts gradually:

Chapter Twelve
On Elevating the Bent Hair from the Eye with a Needle
If the work is on one or two hairs, the way to do it is to take a thin needle and insert into it a fine, soft silk thread, then gather both ends of it and tie them with a very small knot, and let the length of the thread be about four fingers.
Then, sew another thin thread in the glands under it in the fortress, and tie its ends if you wish. The patient places his head on your stone, close to the sun so that the operation can be seen clearly, for it is a delicate procedure. Then, insert the needle through the glands into the root of the excessive hair.
Cut it in the temple with the scalpel until it exits above the natural hair, then pull the thread over the glands and enter through the glands, that hair if it is one, two, or three, no more.
Then, pull with your hand until the hair and glands exit together in the temple along with the natural hair. If the glands are pulled out but the hair is not, pull the glands downwards with the thread that you have sewn into them until the glands exit from the upper hole.
Then repeat with the hair and pull it until it rises in the upper hole, so that the eye sees it. At that point, cut the glands and the thread, and remove them, and close the eye and leave it closed for one or three days, until the hair is firmly placed and the flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add long natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it lengthens after a while, then raise it.
The twelfth chapter.
On the treatment of trichiasis that occurs in the upper eyelid.
The condition that this hair affects is called "hare lip." This hair can be either natural or acquired, and if it is acquired, it results from the healing of a wound, burn, or the like.
The treatment for this involves cutting open the scar, separating its edges, and placing a piece of cotton between them, tying it until healing occurs. It is not advisable to use substances that dry out and contract in its treatment.
For if you do this, the hair will return even more severely than before. Instead, use substances that soften, such as honey and washing with water in which alum has been dissolved along with the cotton seeds of the dyer's broom, mixed with some herbs. Apply this to the affected area and treat it with it.
The defect in its treatment is that it does not return to its original state, and this work we mentioned is only part of correcting the hair so that the place does not return to its previous condition.
"The Fourteenth Chapter

Chapter Fifteen.
In adhesion, fuse the eye with glue or gum.
This adhesion may appear to many people as the glue sticking to the white part of the eye, preventing it from moving easily and hindering its natural function. Therefore, a sharp object should be inserted under the glue.
And it should be lifted up or drawn out with a needle, then the adhesion should be cut with a fine knife that is not sharp like the cutting of knives, but slightly less so that the patient does not get worried during the procedure and the knife might slip and hurt the eye due to its sharpness. Your cutting will be as if you are scraping off that adhesion.
You should wait until the swelling returns to its natural state and all the adhesion is gone, then you should pour salt water into the eye to wash it or drip some of the Zanjari syrup dissolved in water onto it, then separate the swelling from the eye with a cotton pad.
And place a wool pad soaked in egg white over the eye, and after the third day use the dissolved syrups until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen
On cutting the fingernail and removing the flesh of the nailbed.
The nail can be of two types: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard callus, or non-nervous, resembling solid, white wax; if it is touched by iron or pierced with a needle, it cuts off.
And in it, no needle was established, and both types begin from the largest nail to cover a little bit until they block the sight and prevent the movement of the eye, and the method of removing them is that the patient places his head on your pillow.
Then he opens his eyes and you raise the swelling with your hand, then you take the nail with a fine needle and extend it upwards, then you take a needle and insert into it a horse or cow hair, along with a strong thread, and bend the tip of the needle slightly and stitch it in the middle of the nail.
And you execute it with the needle and connect the claw with the thread and extend it above, and remove the hair from the side of the claw that is next to the joint as if you are shaving it with a razor to its end, then cut the rest at the base of the claw with a fine blade or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the claw.
Lest it expose the permanent stream of tears, the claw has been extended with a splint or thread as we mentioned, then smoothed with a soft blade at this edge.
And beware of the scalpel touching the horny layer, as it causes a split and the joint opens up. After cutting, apply some powdered salt or zinc oxide to the eye and leave it for another day, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the claw is insensate and cannot be penetrated by a needle or retain a splint, this type of claw is often treated with medication to heal as described in the section, and if you intend to cut it, open the eye of the patient.
And take a fine, soft blade with a weak edge like this picture. "And I scraped the nail with it, and if you saw it becoming rough and flaky, then sprinkle from your saffron or red safflower or powdered salt on your eye, and close your eye until the next day.

"Chapter Seventeen.
"On cutting the red veins and excess flesh in the eye.
"Sometimes in the eyes of some people, red flesh grows to such an extent that it covers the pupil or approaches it, or flows over the whites of the eye, and sometimes the whites turn outward and resemble a rose.
The work in it is that the patient places his head on your stone, then opens his eyes and removes all that red meat with scissors suitable for this purpose.
Or hold it with tweezers or a hook, then cut the first piece of flesh until all is cut away by pieces, and be careful not to harm the eye during the procedure. Your cutting tool for this should either be a small needle or a small scissor. As you collect with it the sclera as shown in the following illustration for this chapter.
When your cutting is complete and all the roots have been removed, fill the eye with finely ground salt or apply a few drops of any warm-tempered medicine, and place on the eye from the outside a cotton pad soaked in egg white to protect against heat irritation.
But if any root remains and you fear that heat will irritate it, leave the eye and treat it with something to alleviate the irritation. Then return to the operation until it is clear of roots; do likewise with any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this kind.
However, it is advisable for you to avoid treating any weak eye by cautery or by operating with an iron instrument, and do not engage in any of these procedures until you see that the member [the eye] is suitable for this operation.
Chapter Eighteen
On the removal of cataracts from the eye.
The cataract is a red vein that spreads over the eye, preventing vision and weakening the eye with time. Therefore, first examine whether the eye with the cataract is strong and free from other diseases.
When you decide to remove the cataract, instruct the patient to place his head on your lap. Then attach those veins with one or two fine needles, as you see fit, and let the needles be like this figure.
Or let them be two needles fused into one body, like this figure.
Then carefully cut those veins with a fine knife, wiping the blood away bit by bit until you see the eye clear of those veins and free from bleeding. Take care not to harm the eye with the edges of the knife, and perform this operation in the afternoon when the sun is mild.
Be very careful in your operation so that you do not cut anything other than those veins. When you finish, drop some green or red vitriol into the eye to consume any remaining cataract, for if you cannot remove all of it in that moment, the eye will experience pain.
Leave it until her pain subsides and the hot worm becomes safe, then perform on her the operation of this kind with the same intent until she is free from this appearance of the pain of the scissors.
Leave it [and the head of the patient will be in the student's lap, and the rest will wipe away tears and achieve tranquility at the beginning of the eye and behind it, and the root is in the work that the eye should be opened with stitched openings or its triangle, which the student holds].
Cut and the head of the patient will be in the student's lap, and the rest wipe away tears and achieve tranquility at the beginning of the eye and behind it, and you are free to start with a section from the top of the eye or from below it, and the first is to wipe off two nerves in the upper corner, one of which will be at the end of the sclera and the other at its beginning.
Hold it between the middle and the sclera, then tie a nerve from below to both of them, then hold it between the cornea and the iris, and move your hand gently until you see all the veins have been lifted and cut off from the smallest part to the west with scissors if you prefer, or with a gentle pinch.
The suture penetrates its bone and all the passages are cauterized until it rises above the mucus, and do not cut the fleshy part, for tears and blood will gush upon you.
Then cut with the scissors what extends above and below the cornea, so that nothing remains but the conjunctiva at the corner. If you can raise it from the cornea, cauterize with the suture, for this is best. In some people, however, it may ulcerate.
And if you cut it, the pain of his wound will persist for days, and the burning inflammation will intensify; then repeat the procedure on him.

Chapter Twenty
Reattach the protruding eye.
If the eye protrudes with its complete contents and no defect or deficiency in vision occurs, it is necessary to soften the patient, then cauterize him in the upper eyelid, then place a heated cautery in the eyelids without restriction, and anoint it with a fine ointment.
Then place a poultice made of egg whites, cypress, vinegar, and aniseed on the eye. After that, press the upper eyelid with many compresses. The recipe for the compresses is to take plenty of fine ashes; stretch each piece of it over four thin strips made from it.
Then place them one on top of the other according to what the eye needs. Press them with a strong, firm compress and repeat the cautery with ointment without restriction. Remove the cautery and leave the eye compressed for a day and a night.
If the bandage becomes wet during that time, it is necessary to compress gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and night as we said. If you see the eye has returned [to its normal position], otherwise repeat the upper eyelid, compresses, pressing, and cautery until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One
On Cutting the Vulva
If the fistula is in the vulva, and it has broken through to the outside of the labia, resembling a grape in appearance but having the shape of a human, if you intend to cut it off, do as follows:
You insert a needle into the base of the grape from below to above, then you insert another needle in it with double thread from the opposite side and pull it out, leaving the first needle as is.
Then you cut the loop of the double thread and tie part of the grape around the needle, tightening it well. Then remove the needle and place a wool pad soaked in egg white over the area, leaving it until the threads fall out with the grape, then treat the wound to strengthen it.
Until the wound heals, and sometimes the grape is tightened from all sides with a circular ring of leather until the wound swells pleasantly, then you cover it with a thin cloth reaching to the bottom of the wound, so that the egg white oozes out and the wound relaxes and settles in its place. Then you tighten it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two
In treating hemorrhoids.
This is the method for treating hemorrhoids: it is like a swelling that forms in the anus, similar to flowing water but not the same. The way to handle this is to have the patient sit on a stool and then take their head with both your hands.
And move it until you see the swelling go down in your sight, then hold it and it will not protrude again, and light will be released and the patient will see things as he used to see them. If it does not descend, we know that it is water. Therefore, if the swelling does not prepare to descend as mentioned.
Otherwise, have the patient sit between your legs, then take a small piece of thin cloth and make an incision over the corneal skin slightly below where the hemorrhoid meets the corneal skin in the rectum until the swelling comes out.
Then, if it comes out, sprinkle hot water mixed with honey or water in which a lupine seed and honey have been boiled on the anus. After that, continue with the rest of the treatment until he is cured.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
We have mentioned the types of water in the division, and which one is suitable for cupping in a clear and explanatory manner. Therefore, at that time, it is appropriate for the patient to sit between your legs, facing the light, near the sun.
Use your right hand to tie and tighten the healthy eye, then lift its lid with your left hand if the watery eye is the left one, or with your right hand if it's the right eye.
Then place the tip of the cupula near the lacrimal sac with a moderate amount of pressure on the same side of the eye as the smaller conjunctival fornix, then forcefully push the cupula and turn it with your hand until it enters the fornix. You will feel the cupula reaching an empty space.
The depth to which the cupula should reach corresponds to the distance from the palpebral commissure to the end of the lacrimal sac. Since copper can be seen through the clear cornea, the cupula will be visible as it reaches the location containing the water. Then move the cupula upward to the site where the water is located.
Then press it down repeatedly. If the water comes out of its sac, the patient will see what has been opened to his vision from the sac and the cupula in his eye. Wait a moment; if the water rises again, expel it a second time without removing the cupula.
Then, if it has stabilized and does not rise again, remove the cupula gently while carefully turning it with your hand. Then dissolve a little bit of pure rock salt in water and use it to wash the eye from inside. Afterward, place cotton or soft wool on the entire outside of the eye.
To anoint with saffron, rosewater, and egg white, and bind the correct eye with it. As for us and the people of our time, we place crushed caraway seeds mixed with egg white on it. If the cup does not fit in the eye due to its hardness—for there are those whose eyes are extremely hard— It is necessary for the person to take the ointment that is called "buried" and this is its image.
Then pierce with it the same membrane only, and do not mix in the hole, for it is to make a place for the cup to enter gently. Then insert the cup as mentioned, and when you have completed your work, prepare a firm bed for the patient to sleep on his back in a dark room.
He should avoid all movements and coughing, and his food should be prepared in a way that agrees with him. He should not move his head to the right or left suddenly, and the bandage should remain until the third day, then he is released in that dark room, and you test his vision.
Then you show him things, and then you rebind the bandage until the seventh day. It is not appropriate to do this during treatment or after removing the cup from your hand, but rather it is necessary to avoid that before, because water rises quickly with intense gaze.
If the tumor and worm are hot, it is necessary to close the eye before the seventh [day], and this can be achieved by what inhabits the worm until it settles. Then, release his vision and place a mask on his face that protects his sight from beneath for several days while he is in that dark house, then...
Then he exits the house gradually and performs his usual activities. Know that the learner cannot do without observing many cases before performing the procedure himself. I have been informed about some Iraqis who mentioned that in Iraq, they make a porous cup that absorbs water.

Chapter Forty-Two
In the treatment of ulcerated flesh in the nose.
In the nose, various types of excessive flesh may grow. Some resemble polyps with many legs, others are cancerous and raw like blood, and still others are soft and not like blood.
"Whatever of these meats is soft, it is not bad or cancerous. So the patient should sit between your hands facing the sun, and you open the skin and receive the flesh in that meat, then pull it out and cut whatever you can grasp with a very fine, sharp blade from one side.
"Until you know that all the flesh has been removed, if anything remains that you cannot cut, crush it with one of the fine instruments until nothing remains. If bleeding or a hot worm overcomes you.
"If it is suitable or if it is from bad wounds, hurry and cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh disappears. Then pour into the nostril after cutting only water or drink, for opening the nostril and letting the moisture flow to the throat.
"So know that he is cured, for if the moisture does not flow as it should, know that there is a piece of flesh protruding into the upper bones that were not reached by the cutting tool. At this point, you should take a thread from cotton with some thickness.
"Tie many knots in it, and make the distance between each knot as much as a finger or less. The patient should snort the end of the thread into his nostril with a straw or whatever he can manage to make like a straw, pulling the string until it reaches the throat.

Chapter Twenty-Five.
In the soft part at the end of the nose.
Often, a tumor grows at the tip of the nose; it becomes hard and increases in size over the days until it spoils the appearance.
Therefore, it should be cut off as soon as it appears and all of it should be removed; then place on the spot either alkali or a burnt drug that takes the place of alkali. If its cutting is delayed until it becomes hard, look at it: if it is sclerotic with a dark color, it has little sensation.
So do not expose it to iron, for it is a cancerous tumor. Often, when this tumor is cut off, a great ulceration returns in its place on the person. But if the tumor is soft and pale, and I have seen that cutting can be done in all of it. The patient should be emptied and cut without hesitation or fear, and the site should be treated with what dries and closes until it heals.
Chapter Six and Twenty
In the sewing of the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is disrupted due to a wound or similar, know that when the connection in one of these parts is severed, it rarely causes pain except in some people.
When this happens to someone, if the wound is flowing with blood, it is necessary to join the lips of the wound by stitching them together and then treat [them] until they heal. If the separation has already severed its connection and each edge remains intact, it is necessary to cauterize each edge with a hot iron until it heals.
Then join the two lips by stitching them together and compress them, leaving on them powdered resin and milk, and place a patch of glued raisins or other cooked raisins above the suture. Leave it compressed for one to three days, then remove [the patch].
Replace the medicine and leave it until the threads fall out by themselves, then treat [the wound] with resin until it heals. The method of stitching is to join the severed connection either with needles as we have described in abdominal surgery, or with thread as you have learned there.

Chapter Twenty-Nine
On extracting teeth with iron.
There may be rough, ugly scales on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and out, between the gums, which may turn black, yellow, or green until they cause decay to spread to the gums, making the teeth unsightly because of this.
It is necessary for the patient to sit between your hands, with his head on your stone, and you should scrape the molar and the tooth in which you have seen the scales and the material resembling sand until nothing remains of it; likewise, do this with the black, green, and yellow [areas], and others, until they are clean.
If the first scraping has not removed what is in it, then repeat the scraping on another day, second and third time, until you reach your goal. Know that the molar needs many different shaped scrapers of various kinds according to what suits your work.

Chapter Thirty
On the extraction of teeth.
It is necessary to treat the tooth from its pain with every means and to avoid extracting it, so that there will be no consequence if it is extracted, for it is a noble organ. Even if extraction becomes necessary, one should not delay it.
It is necessary, if the patient decides to have it extracted, that he fast until his toothache subsides. Often, the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks it is in the healthy tooth, so he extracts it, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted. We have seen this happen many times with barbers.
If the pain in the tooth subsides, then it is appropriate to loosen the tooth with a thread that has some strength in it so that the gum loosens from all sides, and you move it gently with your fingers or with soft tongs at first lightly until it shakes.
Then you make use of the two large forceps that facilitate well, and place the patient's head between your knees, having secured it so that it does not move. Then draw the tooth towards you, keeping it upright so that it does not break. If it does not come out, take one of those instruments, inserting it beneath from all sides with gentleness and a pin to move it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or eroded, it should be filled with gold, and it should be sealed well with a thin layer of lead so that it does not disintegrate when you press on it with the pliers, and it is necessary to probe around the gum with a needle from all sides.
And be careful not to break it, leaving some of it inside, which will cause the patient more pain than before. Beware of doing what ignorant barbers do when they rush to pull out a tooth without using what we have described.
Therefore, they often cause people great pain; it would be better if the tooth were to break and its roots remained completely or in part. Or if some of the jawbone is pulled out along with the tooth, as we have often seen, then after it is extracted he should chew something like powdered glass and rub it on the site. If there is bleeding from the site, this frequently happens; then grind some of the glass and apply it to the site.
And if the glass does not help, then use the long-handled pliers with a short beak and a wide mouth, so that it does not slip when you grip the tooth with it. This is the shape of the large pliers:
The tweezers should be thick at the tips so that when they are squeezed, they do not give way to themselves or spread out. They should be made of Indian steel or firm steel with precise edges, and on their tips there should be teeth that fit into each other for a secure and strong grip.
And the tips can be made like pincers to also have a strong grip.

Because some diseases have not been mentioned by the ancients, and there is a difference in their types; therefore, if one of the jawbones or any bone in the mouth breaks or decays, search for it in its place using whatever is suitable from among these instruments and the hooks that were mentioned for extracting roots.
And we rely on this image for its appearance.
It will have a little bit of viscosity to control the bone so that it does not slip until the bone comes out. Chapter Two and Thirty.
Concerning the eruption of retained teeth on others.
If the tooth erupts in an unnatural way, it is disfigured by that appearance, especially if this happens in women and children. It is therefore necessary to look into whether the tooth has erupted behind another tooth.
If it cannot be made to erupt or fall out, then extract it; and if it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this tool whose appearance is as follows:
It resembles a small pair of scissors and should be made of sharp Indian steel, so that it can cut through the tooth in many days due to its hardness, without disturbing other teeth. However, if it is possible to extract it, use a forceps from India like this:
It will all be from India, and its engraving will be very fine, it will be like the one used for making needles. You cool the tooth with it a little by little over many days, in order that the tooth does not shake and fall out. Then you soften it further and polish it with some of the polishing stones.
And if a piece of the tooth has broken off and causes pain to the tongue when speaking, it is necessary to also cool this part until the edge of that break disappears, and it becomes smooth and soft so that the tongue does not hurt and speech is not disturbed.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In aligning the movable teeth with silver or gold wires.
If the anterior teeth are shaken or moved by a blow or fall and the patient cannot chew anything for fear that they will fall out, and if drugs that tighten have been applied to them without effect,
The solution is to bind them with a gold or silver wire, and gold is better because silver tends to tarnish and rot after some time, while gold remains in its original state forever and does not experience this. The wire should be of medium thickness and somewhat thick so that it can fit between the teeth.
The figure of the suture is that you take the thread and insert it between the correct teeth, then stitch with both ends of the thread between the mobile teeth, whether one or more, until the stitch reaches the correct tooth from the other side.
Then you repeat the stitch back to the side from which you began, and secure your hand with skill so that the knot does not move, and let your thread tension be at the bases of the teeth so it does not loosen. Then you cut both ends of the extra thread with scissors and gather them together and twist them with a cord and hide them between the correct tooth and the mobile tooth.
So that the tongue does not get caught, then you leave it thus secured, and if it comes loose or breaks, you secure it again with another thread so that it enjoys this condition for the rest of its life. And this is the figure of the teeth and the arrangement of the suture between two correct teeth and two mobile teeth as you see:
Sometimes a single tooth or two will fall out and return to their place, and they are sutured as we have described, and they remain. Only a skillful craftsman can do this. A bone from the bones of an ox can be carved into the shape of a tooth and placed in the spot where the tooth has gone missing. And it will become swollen as we have said, and he will enjoy that.
The Fourth and Thirty-third Chapter.
On cutting the ligament that appears under the tongue to prevent speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may either be natural, with which man is born, or it may be a result of an old wound.
And the treatment is to open the mouth of the patient and place his head on your knee, then raise his tongue and cut that sinewy ligament crosswise until the tongue releases from its hold. If there is some hardness and adhesion in it, being a remnant of an old wound, slice into it with a scalpel and divide it crosswise so that the ligament separates and the knot dissolves.
And be careful not to cut too deep into the flesh, lest you sever a vein there and cause bleeding. Then rinse the wound with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Then place a piece of cotton under the tongue, which the patient holds every night.
Otherwise, if there is bleeding, place powdered alum on the site. If the blood overpowers it, wash the area with a syringe filled with water. Then he treated him with the rest of the treatment until he recovered.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On Extracting the Frog Born Under the Tongue.
Sometimes under the tongue, a worm resembling a small frog prevents the tongue from functioning naturally and may grow so large that it fills the mouth. The treatment for this is to open the patient's mouth in sunlight and observe the worm.
If you see that it is red or black and the patient does not feel it, then avoid touching it because it is cancerous. But if it is inclined towards whiteness and has moisture in it, cut into it with a needle and split it with a fine blade from all sides.
If the bleeding overwhelms you while performing this task, sprinkle some powdered gypsum on it to stop the bleeding. Then continue with your work until you completely remove it. After that, he should rinse his mouth with vinegar and salt.
Then treat him with the rest of the appropriate treatment until he recovers.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
On Treating Worms in the Almonds.
If what grows in the throat from other worms appears as glands inside the throat resembling those that appear externally, they are called almonds. If treated with the methods mentioned earlier and do not heal, then:
Look, if the wound is deep like a bruise and has little sensation, do not expose it to iron, even if its color is red and its base is thick. Do not expose it to iron either for fear of hemorrhage. Instead, leave it until it heals, whether it suppurates or bursts on its own.
If it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is the one that should be cut. The procedure for it is to observe before acting whether the hot wound has completely settled or if part of it is still unsettled.
Then, let the patient sit with his feet in the sun and his head on your knee. Open his mouth and have an assistant press his tongue downwards using this instrument, as shown in its illustration.
It should be made of silver or copper and be as thin as a razor blade. If you press the tongue with it and the wound becomes visible to your sight, take a thread, insert it into the almond-sized hole, and draw it out as much as possible without drawing any other tissue along with it.
Then, cut it with this instrument, as shown in its illustration. It resembles a pair of scissors, except that each of its ends is curved and has sharp edges made from Indian steel or Damascus steel.
If this tool is not available, then cut it with a knife like this image.
One side is sharp and the other is not. After cutting one almond, the others are cut in the same way. Then, after cutting, the patient is washed with cold water or vinegar and water.
If there is bleeding, rinse with water in which pomegranate peels or oak leaves, or similar astringents have been soaked until the bleeding stops, then treat it until it heals. There may also be other wounds that develop around the almonds; they should be cut as described for cutting the almonds.
A woman was treated for a worm that had grown inside her throat, reaching to the Adam's apple with little feeling, almost closing the throat. The woman was breathing with difficulty and was unable to eat or drink water.
She was on the verge of death if she survived a day or two, and the worm had sprouted two branches that exited through her nostrils. So I hurried and inserted a hook into one of them and pulled it out, pulling along with it a piece of flesh.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril opening, then I did that with what appeared from the other nostril opening, then I opened her mouth and pressed down her tongue, then I inserted the needle into the same vein, then I cut some of it, and only blood flowed from it.
So the woman's ring flew off, and she hurried to drink water, then ate some food. We did not stop cutting from that vein for a long time, and the vein would remain instead of being cut until it grew longer with her, and so it was.
The vein widened and was tied inside the ring, and it stopped growing. Then I left that place, and I did not know what happened to her after that.

In cutting the jugular vein.
Which, if it protrudes to the jugular vein and becomes inflamed, is called a 'pillar' when it is white and elongated. If it is thick at the bottom and rounded, it is called an 'aneurysm'. If treated with what we mentioned in the division, the cure will not work.

For it often increases the inflammation, and it is only proper to do this when the heat of the inflammation has subsided. If you do not have this device, take a reed; fit into its end the shell of an egg so that the patient's mouth does not burn.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is a good treatment with its safety.

Chapter Forty.
On the incision and suturing of wounds and their fissures.
Wounds are of many kinds, each differing according to its incision and suturing, as will be mentioned one by one in this book. They vary in two respects: one type is the wound itself with its contained fluids, and the other is...
And the second type is according to the locations in which they occur within the body, for a wound occurring in the head differs from one occurring in the abdomen, and a wound occurring in a fatty area differs from one occurring in a joint. Each of these has its own rules for treatment.
Some wounds should not be incised until the boil in them has ripened and reached its peak, while others should be incised even if they are not yet fully ripe, such as wounds near joints.
Because if a wound occurs near a joint and its condition is prolonged until it becomes gangrenous, the surrounding tissues may become corrupted, affecting the ligaments or nerves of that joint, which can cause paralysis of that limb, or the wound may be near the head of a bone.
You have delayed the removal of the arrow until it is well-healed, thereby damaging the main limb or causing it to be near the joint. Cut it short and only cut the tip that has not healed completely near the joint, lest the pus spreads inside the joint and becomes incurable.
And it will become incurable, and it is important to learn the time of cutting healed wounds that have completely healed. This is when the pain from the wound stops, the swelling disappears, the redness decreases, the pulsations stop, and other signs appear.
And it is necessary to perform the cutting below the location of the wound if possible, so that it will be easier for the pus to drain downwards. Or in a higher location on the wound and more severe swelling, and let the cut be along the length of the body if the wounds are near the hands or feet.
And the locations of muscles, tendons, nerves, and blood vessels in general, in all flat areas without any protrusions; as for those that do protrude, let the cut follow according to that location. But if the wound is in fleshy areas, it is better to leave its cutting intact.
"...so that it solidifies as we have said, if you had sutured it beforehand, the glue from the fish would have been plentiful with pus and putrefaction, and possibly his cheek and jaw could have stiffened, and some of the scars might have been visible on the surface of the body according to necessity or as needed by the limb.
"It is necessary to use a single thread for small scars and a wide thread or many slits according to the size of the scar, and some scars may require that the skin be incised and cut if it has become like a leather.
"It became like the limit of what can be seen in many animals and prisons, some of which are cut with three angles, and others are cut like a plane leaf, such as the crown of an Arabian thorn tree.
"And some of them use circular cuts and crescent-shaped cuts and similar cuts, and if it does not have a head like flat soft scars, then only a simple thread should be used, and if the scar is large and has accumulated much length and has been sutured, do not hurry so that all the pus comes out at that time.
I took some of it out, then pressed the worm for another day, and then took more of the pus as well. Keep doing this gradually until all of it comes out.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, a young child, or an old man with little strength, for the animal spirit often departs with the exit of pus in large quantities, and perhaps the patient will die while you feel nothing. Beware of this matter greatly and be warned.
After removing these worms, it is necessary to wash the wound and look at it. If the worm's cut is small or if the incision is simple, use a bandage of cotton wool or coarse linen. But if the worm is large and the incisions are many...
... it is necessary to insert a bandage into each incision so that they touch each other. And if the worm has been cut from the skin or turned inside out, it must be packed with coarse linen or dry cotton wool and pressed for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate medications until it heals. If blood discharge appears during treatment, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a piece of cotton in them. Apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the abscess persists, it is necessary to use incision, treatment, and urgent measures that we have described in many places in this book. In winter, if the location of the abscess is highly innervated, it is necessary to heat the compresses with hot drink and oil.
And place them on the affected area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, it is necessary to apply compresses soaked in water or oil, or in drink and oil, all cold, until on the third day as we have said, it is necessary to open the abscess and clean it.
And use whatever remedy is needed to heal it, for this is all that is required to know about the general treatment of abscesses. As for the detailed method, each type of abscess has been mentioned with its specific treatment in a summarized manner.
Chapter Forty-One
Concerning the treatment of abscesses that appear in the skin of the head.
Abscesses appear in the skin of the head as small abscesses, and they are one of the types that have pustules. They resemble the yolk of a chicken egg, and their varieties are many, some are fatty and others contain moisture similar to pus.
And among them is what contains moisture resembling Dushish and Hasa, and the like; and some are hardened, solid, and none of it has any danger in its incision or extraction unless you object when extracting a cataract.

And begin the incision from point ~B~ to point ~j~. If the moisture is released, cut the bag that contained that moisture and remove all of it, leaving nothing behind, for often it returns if any part remains.
Then soak a cotton swab in vinegar, or if it is not available, in salt water, and fill the wound with it, leaving it until the next day, for it will dissolve any remaining bag. Then prepare another cotton swab with vinegar, and possibly a third one, as needed.
Until you are certain that nothing remains of the bag, then treat the wound with vinegar until it heals. And if the worm contains an oily substance, incise it as shown in this image:
Cut it with a scalpel and place the hooks in the wound's edges, then scrape it from all sides. If you encounter difficulty in removing the hook, do what I described earlier. Similarly, do this for the worm if it is devoid of the hook.
The treatment is the same as mentioned before, and removing the hook from the worm that has shed its skin is easier because it has less blood and moisture. Some of these worms in the head of certain people have no moisture with them, and I removed a worm from someone who had.

Chapter Forty-Four.

The fifty-fifth chapter.
Cutting into different types of seams.
There are many types of seams, and all their types have been mentioned in the division. Here we should explain the difference between a seam and an abscess, as it is problematic. I say that with an abscess there will be heat, swelling, and severe pain until the pustule softens. And the necrosis completes, at which point the swelling and redness subside. A seam does not have any heat, swelling, or pain, and it is contained in a thin-walled sac that has its own specific space, appearing on the body with a color similar to the skin. Its beginning is like a pimple, then it becomes like a blister, both large and small.
...It is of two types: either fatty or containing moisture, and the colors of moisture are many as mentioned in the classification. Therefore, if you reach the treatment of a wound, it should be delayed and inspected first using the instrument called the scalpel, as shown in the following chapter...
Measure the bone of the ulcer, then remove the scalpel and observe what comes out in its wake. If a viscous moisture of any color exudes, cut it with a simple incision as mentioned for other ulcers. But if no moisture follows the scalpel, know that it is fatty.
Make a firm incision over it as shown in the figure I have described to you, and tie it with ligatures. Scrape the skin from each side carefully, and keep it in a bag if possible, so that it comes out correctly with the wound.
If the bag breaks during the operation and you cannot remove it correctly, it often happens that way. So, extract it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part of it remains, whether small or large, the ulcer will likely recur.
When the wound is deep and only a little of it remains, let the wound be cleansed with some sharp, stringent substances that constrict the wound; place upon it what attracts heat to the wound and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the tissue is large, cut it open and treat it with what adheres.
If you encounter a pulsating or non-pulsating artery with oozing blood, quickly clean the area with powdered alum and leave it compressed for one or two days until the wound heals and the bleeding stops; then return to cutting off what remains of the tissue.

Chapter Seventy-Four
On treating the chest of men.
And the chest of some people may swell during puberty to resemble the breasts of women, and it remains warm and ugly. Therefore, if one dislikes this, it is necessary to scarify the chest with a crescent-shaped scarification tool in this manner.
From line ~b~ to line ~j~ Then scrape off all the fat, and fill the wound with medicinal oil. Then suture the edges of the wound with stitches and treat it until it heals. For if the chest wall extends downward and attaches to the bone as in women.
It is necessary to make two cuts on its upper sides, each in the shape of a crescent, touching each other at their ends until the larger line surrounds the smaller one, as shown in this image: From the line ~b~ to the line ~j~, then scrape off the skin that is between the cuts and remove the fat. Use the stitches and bandages that we have mentioned, along with any necessary treatments, until healing occurs.
If the wound does not absorb as it should, either from fear of the patient or due to blood loss, you must fill the wound with cotton wool soaked in honey-vinegar and leave it until the remaining fat is consumed. Then treat it until healing occurs.

The Ninth and Fortieth Chapter.
On the incision of ulcers that appear before the veins or arteries, known as aneurysms.
If the veins are cut and the skin above it is wounded, an ulcer often appears from that, and likewise, if the artery is wounded, a swelling or ulcer may also appear in it.
The signs by which one can recognize that the ulcer or swelling is from a vein or an artery are as follows: if the ulcer is from a vein, it will be elongated and concentrated in the depth of the body, and if you press on it with your finger, you will feel that it has a pulsation.
And that which is from an artery will be round in the surface of the body. Incising these ulcers is dangerous, especially if they are in the elbow, the wrist, the neck, and many other parts of the body. Great care must be taken not to treat them with iron.
And what is also in the margins or on the head, it should be avoided. If there was swelling of the throat, incise over it in the skin with a long cut. Then open the cut with scissors. Then scrape the throat and clear it from the scabs.
Until it is revealed, then insert a needle under it and thread it to the other side. Then tie the throat with two threads in two places as you were taught for tying the throats of those in the temples. Then cauterize between the ligatures until all the blood in them comes out.
The worm will dissolve, and then the treatment that causes blistering is used until the ligatures fall off. Then it is treated with suitable ointments until he recovers.
If the worm is inside the cut, it is necessary to grasp as much of the worm with the skin as possible. Then insert a needle below where you grasped and thread it so that it comes out on the other side. Then tie it to the worm with a good knot as you were told for tying the grape from both ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire worm at the point where the first needle intersects.
Your threads are attached in four places, then the worm is cut in half until what is inside it comes out; then cut off the excess skin and leave what was connected to it, then place a cloth that has been soaked in vinegar and oil on it. Then use the treatment that is made with pine nuts and grapes until it heals.
Chapter Fifty
The worm that is exposed from the nerve.
Just as the worm is exposed in the veins and arteries, so too is the worm exposed in the nerve if it is struck or occurs due to excessive fatigue, and this happens more frequently in the places of the fingers and toes.
And in each place where the joints move, there is a soft worm that resembles the color of the rest of the body, and it is usually painless. If pressed firmly, the patient feels a sensation similar to a tendon, and the worm does not gather in the depths of the body.
But it is under the skin and moves in all directions, neither forward nor backward. Therefore, whatever is in the joints should not be exposed with iron, for this may cause a delay in healing, and what is in the head or forehead.
Cut the skin with a scalpel, then if the worm is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its root; but if large, ligate it with thread and cauterize it, then remove it and gather the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On cutting the fingers that are exposed in the abdomen.
It often happens to some people that in their abdomen and other parts of the body there are fingers called natural, which resemble a finger in origin; its root is thin and its head is thick. Its two lips may turn into small ones, or they may become very large.
I once saw a man who had in his abdomen two fingers that were almost indistinguishable from an actual finger; they were white and thin-rooted, their two lips having turned into small ones and split open, with moisture constantly oozing out of them. I cut them out, and one weighed about eighteen ounces, the other about six.
The procedure for cutting them is as follows: if the patient is moist and the finger is white, moist, and thin-rooted, cut it with a broad scalpel, and have ready near you a hot iron, because often much blood will flow upon their being cut.
If you are bleeding, bandage it. If you see the patient is anxious and afraid of cutting with iron, take a thread from lead wire and tighten with it the vein that has this description, and leave it for two days, then increase in the tightening of the lead, do not stop tightening the lead.
Whenever you touch the vein until it breaks and falls out on its own, without effort, if the vein is thick at its root, there may be some fear and hesitation in cutting it, especially when it is in the abdomen, but part of it or half of it can be cut.
Then bandage it so that it does not return, then treat it until the wound heals. Be careful not to cut a vein that is dark in color, has little sensation, and looks dull, for it is a sign of cancer. And we will mention cancer later.
Chapter Two and Fifty.
In the treatment of bladder stones.
The cause of bladder stones can be numerous, either from the tearing of the fascia covering the abdomen, which releases blood or urine into the rest of the cavity, or from blood that oozes from a vein or artery, as mentioned before, or from air that has been trapped.
And if it is before the rupture of the abscess and the exit of the pus, then its color is similar to that of the flesh, and it will be soft without pain, and various positions will appear. And if it is before the exit of the urine, its condition will be more varied with what we have described, so that when you press it with your fingers, it disappears then returns. It may sometimes be accompanied by a rumbling sound and greatly increases at bathing or heavy labor. If it is before wetness, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then along with these signs, the pus will appear black. If it is due to congealed meat, the pus will be yellow and firm, and it will settle in one place.
If it is due to wind, its touch will be soft. The treatment for this is to observe, if the vein of the anus is affected by blood or pus or wind, then you should abstain from treating it, as there is fear and doubt in that, as I have informed you in the chapter where I mentioned the abscesses that occur due to the veins and pus.
"For if the hernia is caused by water or cold, it is necessary to instruct the patient to grasp himself and stand erect, then learn to bandage around the hernia completely. Then instruct him to lie down on his back, then bind around the hernia with a wide bandage in the place you learned to bind, then pass through the middle of the bandage above with a large needle, then tie the binding place with a strong thread or thick silk securely.
The binding knot should be a series of loops, then open the middle of the bandage above the knot and insert your little finger, and ask for water. If you find it taken by the bandage, loosen the loops and push the water into the abdomen. And if cold is found, draw it out with a needle.
And cut its tip, for if you find resistance from the intestine or vein, then bandage it firmly and return to your work. Then take two needles and insert in them two strong threads; insert the two needles into the binding that you made around the hernia with two swabs that have passed through them, then press the hernia in four places on the needles.
If you wish, remove the thread and leave the place until the compressed flesh decomposes and falls off by itself, or cut it if it softens and decomposes, then treat it with appropriate medicines until it heals, for it was a piece of flesh that was not completely removed.
Or if it is due to moisture, it is necessary that the worm should be cauterized as we mentioned and the blood or the moisture that you find in it should be evacuated, then treat it with what can heal the place.
Chapter Thirty-Five
On the Treatment of Cancer
We have mentioned in the division the types of cancer and how to treat it with medicines, and we warned against treating it with iron lest it ulcerate. We also mentioned cancer that occurs in the uterus and warned against its treatment.
The earlier discussions mentioned that when the cancer is in a place where it can be completely removed, such as breast or thigh cancer and similar removable organs, especially if it starts small; but if it progresses to become large, it should not be approached.
For I could not heal anyone from it, nor have I seen anyone else who achieved this before me, and the procedure in it, if it is feasible as we mentioned, should be advanced so that the patient can escape the black day, then it will putrefy him clearly if it is in the veins.
Then place the patient in a position suitable for surgery, then obtain the lancets used for cancer, then cut it from all sides with the skin until its roots are completely removed, leaving the blood to flow and not cutting it off quickly.
On the contrary, compress the wound and clot all the thick blood either by hand or with any available instruments. If you encounter heavy bleeding from cutting veins or arteries, cauterize until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the medication until he is cured.

Because when you treat them together, the vagina may perforate and it may also present in women with gangrene or putrefaction of the vagina. It is necessary to cut out all that has turned black and seems about to putrefy or has already putrefied.

This adhesion only occurs in those whose foreskin is normal and circumcision was not necessary for them, and it can occur due to a wound or bite. It should be treated with a potent caustic until the connection dissolves and the foreskin separates from both sides.
If it is difficult to separate them with a scalpel, then some of the foreskin and the glans should be cauterized because the glans is thin and may tear easily due to its delicacy.
