Chapter Seventy-Five.
On Purifying Children and Treating Their Ailments.
Circumcision is not something other than the separation of connection, as in all other surgical procedures, except that since it is done by our will and used specifically for children, we must describe the best method for it.
And the easiest way leading to safety, so I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not practiced among their laws, and it is what we have acquired.
Through experience, because I have found that most of the surgeons and cupping practitioners use circumcision with a scissor or a knife, and they use the ligature with thread and cut with the nail, and I have tried all these methods.
Therefore, I did not find anything better than circumcision with a knife and ligature with thread because circumcision with scissors often leads to loss of skin due to the fact that the foreskin has two layers, so it may cut off the outer layer while the inner layer remains.
Cutting further, persistent pain, and cleansing with a probe are not reliable for cutting the end of an ulcer, as it may enter its cavity. As for cleansing with a nail, there is a risk that the skin will slip and spoil your work, or the child's skin may be naturally short.
Many are born this way and do not need cleansing, as I have seen. As for cleansing with scissors and suturing with thread, experience has shown me its preference because the scissor blades correspond to each other like the upper blade corresponds to the lower blade.
When you have squeezed your hand so that the two blades of the scissors cut at once and at the same time, the stitch becomes like a wall around the ulcerous skin from all sides, without any mistake in suturing. The first step is to make the child faint.
Especially if he is one of those who understands little that you are only tying the thread in his ulcer and leaving it for another day, then you make him happy and glad in every way possible with him, according to what his mind accepts. Then you stop him between your hands while standing upright.
He should not be sitting, and hide the scissors in your pocket or under your foot, lest the child's eye fall upon it among the tools. Then insert your hand into the dilator and blow into the skin, flattening it until the tip of the dilator comes out.
Then clean it from the gathered pus, then tie a double thread to the marked spot, then tie another thread a little below it, then hold the two ends of the threads and the scab with the lower knot firmly, and cut between the two threads. Then quickly lift the skin and remove the dilator, then let the blood flow for a bit, which is better and causes less inflammation of the dilated area. Then wash it with a wet rag, then sprinkle on it some dry, burned ashes or also fine semolina flour, both are excellent.
Then quickly lift the skin and remove the dilator, then let the blood flow for a bit, which is better and causes less inflammation of the dilated area. Then wash it with a wet rag, then sprinkle on it some dry, burned ashes or also fine semolina flour, both are excellent. Then place on the ulcer from above a piece of white linen cloth cooked in rose water mixed with good-quality rose oil, and leave it on until the next day. Then continue treating it with the rest of the treatment until he is cured.
This is the depiction of the scissors suitable for cauterization.
The blade should be sharp without a swivel on the axis, and the length of the blades should be equal to the handles. As for errors in cauterization, it may happen that all or part of the inner skin is flipped when cut; you must extend it with your fingernail before the site becomes hardened.
Cut it evenly; if you are unable to grasp it with your fingernail, attract it with tweezers and cut it. If three days have passed and the swelling beneath the head of the boil remains hot and red, leave it until the inflammation subsides, then scrape it gently with a spoon.
Cut it according to what is convenient for you, and take care not to cut the head of the boil; if part of the head is cut off, it does no harm. Treat it with any of the ointments we have described in our chapter on ulcers.
If you cut more skin than needed and it is raised above the surface, this also does not cause much harm. Treat it with what we have mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Eighty-Five
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Urine retained in the bladder is due to its blockage by a stone, clotted blood, or swollen flesh, and the like. If it is treated with what we mentioned for the number of treatments described in the division.
If the urine has not been released and it is observed that its retention is due to a stone that has obstructed the neck of the bladder, the patient should lie on his back and then another person should sit on his stomach, pressing his abdomen towards his back while the patient himself holds onto something as much as he can.
Then, the neck of the bladder will be forced to expel the stone towards the back and the urine will be released. But if it does not come out with what we mentioned and the condition worsens for the patient, it is necessary to use an instrument called a catheter, which looks like this:
It is made of silver and should be thin, smooth, and hollow like the beak of a bird in the length of a cubit, about half a finger long. One end has a delicate bulb. To draw out the urine with it, one should take two threads and tie them firmly at the end with wool or cotton.
The thread's end enters below the anus, and a small piece of wool is used as a stopper to prevent it from entering the bowel, like a plug. Then, the anus is anointed with oil, lard, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
His penis and scrotum are anointed with wet adhesive, oil, or thick water. Then, the thread is inserted into the anus accompanied by the enema until it reaches the bottom of the anus. The anus is then turned upwards towards the side, and the thread is pushed inside.
Until, when the anus is turned, the thread is pushed inside until it approaches the seat. Then, the penis is tilted downward with the thread inside it, and it is pushed until it reaches the anus, and the patient feels that it has reached something empty.
It is done in this manner because the urethra, through which urine flows, has a twist. Then, the thread is pulled with some wool tightly, and the urine follows the wool. After it is removed, urine will flow out, and the thread is reinserted.
Do not continue to do that until the bowels are emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter Ninety-Five.
How to Enema with a Syringe.
And the figure of the instrument that can be used for this is made of silver or hollow bone with a long tube on the end of a syringe, and it was intended to inject water and drugs into it; this will be done by an instrument called the syringe, and here is its image:.
It is made of silver or hollow bone with a long tube on the end of the syringe, and it is all hollow except for the tip, which is sealed. There are three holes in it, two from one side and one from the other, as you see.
The hollow place where the piston is located should be closed without any excess so that when something liquid is drawn with it, it will draw; and when it is pushed, it will push to a distance similar to the catapult used for throwing oil in sea wars.
If you want to inject liquid into the bowels, insert the tip of the syringe into the liquid and draw it up with the piston until the liquid is drawn into the hollow of the syringe. Then insert the tip into the anus as we have described for the enema and push the liquid out with the piston.
The moisture reaches the anus while standing, so that the patient feels it, and this is also a fine injection for the anus.
It is made from silver or brass, its top part resembling a small funnel, and below it there is a ring in which the band passes. Then take an animal's rectum, put into it the moisture you want to inject into the rectum, then tie it firmly between the two rings with a double thread.
It is warmed slightly over the fire, then its tip is inserted into the anus, and you press your hand firmly on the anus with the moisture until the patient feels that the moisture has reached the anus.

Then tie it to the instrument and use it as you would with a rectum, pressing it with your hand until the moisture reaches the anus.
Chapter Sixty.
Removal of the stone.
We have mentioned in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medications, and we noted the difference between the stone that forms in the kidney and the stone that forms in the bladder, along with all its signs. We also mentioned the stone on which manual operation and cauterization are performed.
And these are specifically the stones that form in the bladder and that grow in the urethra, and I have described this clearly and concisely.
So I say that the stone that forms in the bladder is more common among children, and one of its signs is that urine exits from the bladder resembling water in its flow.
And sand appears in it, and his penis becomes constricted and he plays with it, and often it is delayed, then spreads, and with it the desire for defecation appears in many of them. It is easy to heal children from this until they reach fourteen years old, but it is difficult for the elderly, and as for adolescents, it is moderate between these two. ︎
And the one whose stones are larger, his treatment will be easier, and for smaller ones it is the opposite. So if we proceed to treatment, firstly it is necessary that the patient is injected with an enema that expels all the stool in his intestines, for it may prevent the stone from being found during the examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet and shaken downwards so that the stone descends into the bladder, or he may be made to jump from a high place several times, then you sit him between your legs, with his hands under his thighs, so that the entire bladder tilts downwards, then examine him.
Then you make him urinate from the outside, and if you feel the stone in the urethra, hurry to press on it, for if it does not fall out, you should moisten your little finger with oil from your left hand if the patient is a child, or your middle finger.
If he is an adult, insert it into his urethra and search for the stone until when it falls under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder, then press on it with your finger and push it out to the desired location to be crushed.
And you command a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and another servant to stretch the membrane with his right hand and with his other hand to stretch the skin below the membrane away from the location where the crushing will occur, then take the instrument that looks like this:
Cut between the chair and the anus, not in the middle but towards the left side of the anus, and the cut should be on the same surface as the chair and your finger in the anus, and you press it outwards, making the cut wide from the outside and narrow from within, to the extent that the stone can come out.
Do not press the finger too hard in the anus, lest it push out the stone before the incision is made, and know that sometimes stones have corners or edges which make their removal difficult.
Some are soft, like clay, and rounded, making their removal easy. If it has corners or edges, make the incision a little larger; if it does not come out this way, it is necessary to use a tight band around it, either by gripping it with a firm clamp whose end is like a spoon.
To secure the stone so it does not escape, either use a delicate instrument with a hooked end inserted beneath it, or if you cannot reach it, widen the incision slightly; and if there is any blood, cut it off with scissors.
If there are more than one, first push the larger one into the rectum and then cut on it; afterwards, push the smaller one and do likewise if there are more than two. If they are extremely large, it is better not to make a large incision on them because this exposes the patient to risk in two ways.
If he dies or if he has constant dribbling of urine, it is because the place does not heal. Try to push it out until it exits or dissolves in its breakage with blades until it comes out completely.
When you have finished treating the wound with candles, vinegar, patience, bandaging it tightly and covering it with a cloth soaked in oil, wine or almond oil and cold water to soothe the burning, then let the patient lie on their side and do not change the dressing until the third day.
If it becomes inflamed, wash the area with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with fig paste and a poultice until it heals. If excessive heat, pain or similar symptoms appear in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder preventing urination and recognizable by the presence of blood in the urine.
Then insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will cause the bladder to rot and deteriorate. Afterwards, wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, or whatever treatment method is suitable until it heals.
It is necessary during all treatment times to bind the thighs together and gather them to secure the medicines placed on the site, for if the stone is small and goes into the urethra and sticks in it and prevents urination from exiting, treat it with what I will describe before it reaches the narrow part.
Often, this treatment is sufficient without reaching the narrow part; I have tried this myself, which is to take a hook made of steel shaped like this:
A triangle with a pointed edge, pierced in the anus, then take a thread and tie it around the urethra below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder. Then insert the hook into the urethra until it reaches the same stone.
Then turn the hook a little in the same stone until you have made an opening through it, and then it will exit from the other side; immediately urine will flow out. Keep your hand on what remains of the stone outside the urethra; it will crumble and come out with the urine, and the patient will be cured.

Then, the bandage is changed and the clotted blood in the wound is cleaned out. The thread must be tied below the stone to prevent it from returning to its original place; the other end of the thread is tied above so that if the thread breaks after the stone is removed, the skin will return to its place and cover the wound.
And for that reason, if you tie the upper thread, you should raise the skin upwards so that when you release it, it will return and cover the wound as we have said.

The ninetieth chapter
On hemorrhoids
Hemorrhoids are forbidden in our religion.

The kangaroo, the wallaby, the cat, and similar animals, I say that cauterization is done in two ways: either by searing or by incision and cutting. The method of searing is to sit the animal in hot water until its female opens up and softens.
Then you cauterize it with your hand until it softens and is not painful to touch. As for the method of incision and cutting, it is necessary to grasp the animal and press its skin with your left hand. Then tie a tourniquet and make an incision on each testicle once.
until the testicles protrude, then cut them off after cauterizing them. Do not leave anything of the vessels except for the thin vessel that is on the epididymis. This method of cauterization is better than that which is done by searing.

Fetuses in the womb sometimes fall out, they may be one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, more than ten, and I have seen a woman who gave birth to seven, and another who gave birth to fifteen, all of them were seen like this.
By the estimation of the noble and knowledgeable, all this should be part of the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps something like that may happen to her one day, so she will meet it as is necessary.
Chapter Sixty-Seven
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus
If the dead fetus was not expelled using the previously mentioned treatment methods, and you are forced to use your hand, then it is necessary to observe whether the woman has normal strength and does not have any conditions that could lead to harm from this procedure. In such a case, the woman should lie down on her back on the bed.
The head of the woman will be tilted downwards, with her legs raised and held by two assistants from both sides or tied to the bed so that her body does not move during delivery.
Then, her mouth is moistened with wet cloths along with the root of sweet almond, rosewater, and cotton seeds. Then, she anoints her right hand with these cloths and ointments, then inserts it into the womb gently, then seeks a place to pierce the amniotic sac from the fetus and the suitable places for that.
If the baby is presenting head first, pierce the amniotic sac in its eyes, nostrils, mouth, navel, under the chin, or any of the nearby places to the shoulders or under the armpits.
If it is descending with its feet first, then in the ankles and the middle of the thighs. It is appropriate to hold the amniotic sac with the right hand and roll it between the fingers of the left hand, then gently insert the hand and pierce the sac in some of those places we have mentioned.
Until she reaches something empty, then pierces another sac or a third one, if needed, to ensure that the suction is even and does not tilt in any direction. Then, she extends the extension steadily, not just vertically but moving it in all directions.
It is not easy to extract it as it is done with the tooth when extracted, and between that it is necessary for the cloth to be moistened; if something remains stuck, it is necessary to anoint some fingers with oil and insert them from the side to direct what is stuck. If some of the contents come out, move the tweezers to other places.
Raise it slightly, do this until all the contents come out. If a hand comes out before the others and cannot be returned due to its constriction, it is necessary to cut a slit in it, then draw it out until it all comes out, then cut it off.
Whether in the wrist or the ankle, you should do the same with the other hand and the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and feels pressed during birth, or if there is water collected on its head, it is necessary to insert a hook between the fingers.
And tear the head with it, and the water will come out, or cut it with the instrument called the scissors, whose image comes with the images of all the instruments in the chapter following this one.
Likewise, if the fetus has a large head by nature, it is necessary to tear open the fontanelle or cut it with scissors as we said, then extract the bones with forceps. If the head comes out and is constricted at the neck, tear until the moisture in the chest subsides.
When the chest adheres, if it does not adhere and does not approach for exit, cut as much as you can. If it is below the abdomen or has a tumor, it should be opened until the moisture comes out of it.
If the fetus is on its feet, extracting it is easy and placing it in the uterine mouth is simple. However, if there is pressure on the abdomen or chest, it is necessary to extract it with a hook wrapped around your hand and open the abdomen or chest until what is inside them comes out.
If all the limbs are removed and the head has retreated, then let the left hand enter. If the uterine mouth is open, let the hand enter into the neck of the uterus and request the head with it, and draw it to the uterine mouth with the fingers; then introduce a hook or two hooks and draw it with them.
If the uterine mouth is adhered to a hot dry wound, it should not be torn but rather, at that time, wet and dirty substances should be used and pessaries employed; sit in water that boils and becomes softened.
And if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to turn it, then use what we mentioned for the living fetus; if not, cut and remove it, and nothing of the placenta should be left inside the uterus. Once you have finished treating, use what we mentioned for the treatment of hot wounds that occur in the womb.
If there is a flow of blood, it should be submerged in cold water and the rest of the treatment used. I have seen a woman who became pregnant and her fetus died inside her, then she became pregnant again.
Then the other fetus also died, and after a long time an ulcer appeared on her vulva and swelled until it burst and discharged pus. So she was called to be treated, and she was treated for a long time, but the wound did not heal. Therefore, some strong adhesive plasters were applied to it.
Then a bone came out of the place, and after several days another bone came out. I was surprised by this, as there are no bones in the womb. So I thought that these were bones from the dead fetus. Therefore, I explored the wound and found many skull bones coming out of it.
I have not ceased to follow this until I extracted many bones from her, and the woman is in good health, and she lived thus for a long time, and indeed I came here with this rare example.
For it contains knowledge and assistance for what the physician attempts by hand in treatment.

Chapter Eighty-Seven.
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus.

Then enter it into the womb and search for the membrane, and if it catches it, hold onto it and extend it little by little until you remove it. So that the womb does not drop during strong pulling, it should be moved gently to the right and left sides, then increase the amount of pulling, for it will come out at that time and free itself from adhesion. And it will dissolve and come out, except that when it rots, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and the head, and this troubles the patient. Therefore, it is necessary to use a suitable powder.
The first ones have tried some herbs, such as the leaves of the hemp plant and dried figs, to induce abortion in women experiencing delayed menstruation or menstrual blockage.

Chapter Ninety-Seven.
In the treatment of fistulas other than perforated ones.
Many children are born with fistulas that are not perforated, and the opening is often closed by a thin membrane. It is appropriate for the midwife to puncture this membrane with her finger or, if she cannot, to tear it with a sharp hook, taking care not to damage the muscle.
Then she should place a wool swab soaked in water and oil on it, and then treat it with compresses until it heals. If she fears that it may close again, she should insert a lead tube into the opening for several days and remove it when the child tries to defecate.
The closure of the fistula may also result from a scar or a wound, and it is appropriate to incise that scar and then treat it as we have mentioned in the treatment and cauterization methods.
Chapter Eighty.
In the treatment of fistulas that occur below.
The fistulas that occur below are adhesions and thickenings which take place near the anus, either outside or inside one of the areas, and there is one fistula or more.
If that adhesion becomes severe, it opens and discharges a watery white liquid or thin pus. Some of these fistulas may extend to the bladder and the rectum, while others are not extended. The extended ones can be recognized by what is discharged from them in terms of odor and smell when the patient uses the toilet.
Sometimes worms come out of them, and if they are inside the body, they may extend to the bladder and the urethra. Some may extend to the joint of the thigh or the gluteal fold. What is known as a fistula that extends to the anus can also be non-extended.
Insert your little finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula if there is no discharge in it. If there is discharge, insert into it a thin leaden probe or a horsehair.
"Until you feel the thread or hair in your finger, if it does not cause pain and nothing emerges from the hole - neither pus, nor smell, nor worms as we mentioned - then know that it is not healed, so hurry to treatment.
"If the abscess is healed to the anus or the urethra, its sign is the discharge of urine from it and its refusal to be sealed by medicines. However, if it is healed to the joint of the thigh or the tip of the penis, its sign is that the thread reaches there.
"If it does not have a tunnel to the bone, and there is pain towards the area, and pus constantly comes out of it, and no treatment heals it, nor does it seal with cauterization, then all these healed abscesses are not free from pain.
"Treating it is a vain hope for those who are fooled by ignorant doctors. As for those that are not healed and not suppurating, it is hoped that they will heal with the use of iron, as I have described and tested. This involves making the patient lie on their back between your hands.
"His leg should be raised above him and his thigh tilted towards his abdomen, then a copper or lead thread is inserted into the abscess if there is a tunnel. If the patient feels it towards the anus, it is necessary to insert your probing finger into the anus.
If you feel that the splinter in your finger has penetrated on its own and is exposed, but you do not feel it between your finger and the object with a membrane or flesh, then know for certain that it has penetrated. Do not waste effort trying to remove it, as there is no benefit from it, as we have said.
They say that in some people it heals on its own rarely, and one of the treatments hoped to benefit is to apply a thin wick over the capacity of the syringe as was previously mentioned, and insert a plug into the syringe until it reaches near the seat, then repeat it two or three times.
So that you know it has completely burned all of the excess fat that resembles a bird's quill, then treat it with an ointment in oil until that fat comes out, and then treat it with cooked meat, and if it heals, it is considered cured.
And if the splinter did not penetrate to the finger in the seat and there was a thick membrane or flesh between it and the splinter, and you saw the syringe on the surface of the skin, then at that time tear the skin from the first part of the syringe. You pass through the fleshy part with the probe until you reach the end of the probe with the fleshy part, and the probe is released and falls off. Then you clean that soft flesh that resembles a bird's quill, and nothing remains of it.
Then you treat it with hot cauteries until it is healed, and if blood overcomes you and hinders your work, it is best to cut off that flesh and the fastest benefit is to burn it with fire or a sharp instrument, because burning with fire achieves two good results: burning away the excess flesh.
And cut the blood and dry it, then treat it with pitch boiled in oil or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until it forms a scab, and that burned flesh comes out with the scab. Then treat the wound at that time with hot cauteries suitable for firm flesh.
And these are the drugs that have astringent and drying properties, for if it heals, it has been cleansed and there is no need to be ashamed of recurrence. But if the ulcer extends to the depth of the seat and away from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the seat and examine it with that.
If you feel the arrow and between you and it is a barrier of leather or flesh, and it was close to exiting, there is no action except by hope and expectation; this is because you should use one of three methods: either you pull it out as we said.
And if you pierce it until you reach its tip, so that you can introduce the stylet and treat it from nearby, do not hesitate in piercing, lest you cut the ligament surrounding the seat, causing the barb to exit unintentionally; then treat him as we mentioned. Perhaps he will heal, as we said.
And if you penetrate that barrier and then bore it after piercing it close to the seat, either with the arrow or another sharp tool, then clean all the contaminated flesh in the bore as much as possible.
Then stuff the entire wound, along with the bore and the upper mouth, until it is sealed, leaving only the barb open inside the seat, which will be lighter on the patient, and the barb may split in this manner.
If the arrow enters through the barb and is in one side of the seat near the surface of the body, along with the skin and the rim of the seat, take at that moment an arrow with a sharp tip like a needle's point in this manner:.

Chapter Seventy-One
On the veins from which blood is drawn, their cutting and treatment of ruptures.
The varices are in two forms: either they are inside the cushion, resembling grape seeds or like clusters of raisins, and they are small and large, and blood is constantly oozing from them.
They can be one or many, and they can be outside the cushion and on its edges. However, those that are outside the cushion are usually less moist, from which a yellowish liquid or a small amount of blood constantly oozes, and they match the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the cushion is to instruct the patient to protrude and protrude until the cushion exits, and then you see the small intestine. You must hurry to tie it with silk thread or hold it with your fingers, then cut it at its base. If the silk threads do not catch due to their moisture and smoothness.
Then wrap it with a coarse cloth or a piece of rough linen, or pull it with your fingers, then cut it. After cutting, sprinkle some harsh astringents on it so that it holds its position, or pack it as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat him with the rest of the treatment until he recovers. If the chair does not suit you for sitting, then inject the patient with a syringe to feel some pain, so that it washes away what is in the chair and urges for exiting quickly when the patient shows signs of improvement. The external hemorrhoids are easier to manage; just give them an easy command.
And it is to take them with your fingernails or hook them with tweezers and cut them, then treat them with what was mentioned until they heal. If one dislikes cutting with iron, it is necessary to use their nails for this purpose, taking a thin thread and inserting it into the needle.
Then pull the hemorrhoid up and pierce it with the needle from below on the other side, and leave both ends of the thread hanging below the needle while tightly gripping the hemorrhoid. After tying the thread and removing the needle, do this for all the hemorrhoids except one, which should not be perforated.
To drain blood from it, then place a moistened bandage soaked in rose or cypress oil on the seat and put it on the seat and order the patient to rest, then leave him until they fall off. If the hemorrhoids have fallen off, treat them with compresses and the rest of the treatment until they heal.
And as for the treatment of fissures, it often presents with hardness of the edges and clotting of blood. So if it becomes urgent and does not heal with medication, then it is necessary to scrape it with a knife or your nail until it becomes soft and separates from its top layer that prevents it from healing.
Then treat it until it heals as needed. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more vigorously than before until it bleeds and becomes inflamed. Then treat it again; it will heal.
Chapter Two Hundred and Eighty-Four
In the treatment of prolapsed and non-prolapsed hemorrhoids, dry ulcers, and gangrene.
The hemorrhoid is only a round knot, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, which occurs in all parts of the body and especially in the soles of the feet and the fingers. It causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the hemorrhoid and hold it with a hook or forceps, then pull it out from its root. Then treat it, and if you wish, apply a cautery on what was mentioned in its section. As for dry ulcers, one of them is a small, rough, round, and hard nodule on the surface of the body.
And often it appears in the hands of children, and its treatment is to lengthen the vein and cut or tear it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls out. If you wish, you can cauterize it with fire or with a strong medicine.
And the inverted and uninverted veins can be removed with the instrument that I mentioned for treating moles. As for the mole, it is also a small swelling with thick fluid on the surface of the body that goes deep into the body, and if it is affected by cold, it feels like the sting of an ant.
And it can be found anywhere in the body, but most often it is found in the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from the hair of a goat or the strong hair of an eagle and to place this tube around the mole until the circle of the tube encompasses its edges. Then you rotate your hand with the tube until what surrounds the mole is torn.

Chapter Forty-Eight.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification how to treat what resembles ulcers, and now I am mentioning here their complete treatment by hand and with medicines. I say that wounds differ according to what the wound is on and according to where it occurs.
The things that wounds can be on are numerous, such as a thorn, stone, sword cut, knife cut, spear thrust, arrow or similar things.
And as for the wounds according to their locations on the body, there are those that occur on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
And I will describe the treatment of some wounds so that it may serve as a measure and law for other wounds, and I will begin with simple head wounds because complex ones will be mentioned in the chapter entitled "Thirty-Eight" in this book.
"I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head and no bone is broken, I would look to see if it was from a stone or something similar, and if only the skin was torn, and the wound was large, and there was concern about hot swelling in the patient, then I would quickly remove the bowel onto the place. "And do not delay that, and draw blood from him according to his strength if there is no obstacle preventing removal, especially if the bleeding from the wound is profuse, let the removal be against the direction of the wounded part.
"And warn the patient against overeating, drinking, and thick foods, for preventing the removal is an obstacle; so let him eat little food, not approaching meats or beverages, and place on the wound if there occurs with it hot swelling, a linen cloth soaked in rose oil alone or with the drink that has honey.
"And if the hot swelling is controlled, place on the wound if it is oozing blood and the wind has not altered it, which I will describe to you shortly, but if the wind has altered it, place some linen cloths on it.
"Until the bowel is fully grown, then treat it until it heals. And if the wound is large and from a sword or similar, and its edges have not come together with the sutures, then join them by stitching as I will describe in the chapter on abdominal wounds.
If the skin is exposed and hanging by a thread, and its attachment is only to loose tissue that can be cut, then cut it off, and treat the wound with medicines that will form firm flesh in place of the skin. And instruct the patient to eat foods that contain fat, such as heads and brains, and similar foods.
If there is a wound in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, cut off all the detached part and suture it back together. Then treat it, for if these wounds have blood vessels or veins from which blood flows and the bleeding is not stopped by medicines, then explore those blood vessels.
If it (the wound) did not heal, cauterize it with the cautery, bind it, and if necessity demands it, even if what we mentioned does not help, burn it until the bleeding stops. For these wounds are small and simple; their treatment is easy and sufficient by leaving this cloth on them while they are still bloody.
Before the air changes it, and the recipe for the cloth is to take a piece of milk and two pieces of oil and three parts of either hot or cold lime. Grind all together and sift, then bandage the wound with it and bind it tightly until it heals well.
He was bandaged with blood, then wrapped from above with cotton cloths and left until he did not need to be changed again; for the medicine will not come off of him until he is cured. Lime powder alone can do this if you do not have yogurt and oil available.
And this can also be done with eggs in many of the larger wounds, if you are skilled in applying it and bandaging them, and the wounds are fresh with blood that has not changed due to the air; but if they have been altered somewhat by the air, especially in the summer season.
There is no benefit in putting this on unless you treat it as we mentioned, by placing some boiled egg yolks on it or a poultice of wheat flour made with water and honey until the swelling subsides, then continue treating it with the rest of the treatment until he is cured.
But if the wound is accompanied by a fracture in the bone and it protrudes, draw it back with a cord. The treatment of fractures has been mentioned as needed in head wounds, so there is no difference in treating a neck wound compared to a simple head wound.
If a nerve from the neck or vein is cut, if it is a nerve, there is no treatment for it except to avoid treating it with anything that constricts strongly like a chain and a ring, because they irritate the nerve and make it throb.
And do not place anything cold on the wound, because the core of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest organ, will cause what you treat it with from soft medicines like washed rose oil mixed with water many times, soaked in oil or rose oil and lavender.
If it is also washed with pure water and other mineral stones of this kind, and moist compresses; and if the wound is large, use stitching or close its edges with strips of linen, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a cavity and there is a hidden space beneath it where pus has gathered, then open it in its deepest part; and if a vein is cut in the wound and its blood flows out, then tie it or bandage it, if necessity requires it.
If the wound has cut some of the tendons or all of them, and the fingers are paralyzed, gather the edges of the skin by stitching over the tendon, but do not touch the tendon; rather, smooth it out and return it to its natural shape, then bind it with a firm bandage and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with medicated poultices that have compression and drying until he is cured. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are splinters of bone, remove them all or as much as possible. Whatever you cannot remove, leave until the wound heals and cures, and then extract it.
If the wound is deep, apply the bandage we described earlier. If bone appears in the wound and it becomes prominent, treat it as described in the chapter on wounds of the chest and between the shoulders.
If the wound is from a spear or sword and you see it has an opening, observe if air comes out when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a penetrating wound. If there is no opening and the wound is deep with blood, do not put a bandage in it at first, nor tighten it, lest the blood be trapped inside.
But bring the wound to the heart, and it will kill the patient. Instead, place two absorbent medicated poultices in it and change them twice a day. If you do not have a medicated poultice, put a cotton wick in the mouth of the wound to absorb the moisture coming out of it, and let the patient sleep on the wound.
The wound is safe if, even after three days or more have passed since the injury and there are no signs of infection such as swelling, darkening, or difficulty in breathing, and if all other conditions appear normal; then know that the wound is healed. Treat it with cauterization and the rest of the treatment until recovery.
If the urine is difficult to pass and the flow is continuous, know that it has become obstinate; treat it from its opening. The wound is safe if, even after a chest or back wound has passed, there are no signs of infection in it; then treat it with suturing as previously mentioned.
If the fragment is large or in the urethra if it is small, examine the wound and be quick to remove those fragments if they are free; if not, leave them until.
It will soften the wound, making it easier to remove them; as for other wounds occurring in the rest of the limbs, their treatment is according to what we have mentioned.
Chapter Fifty-Eight
On Abdominal Wounds, the Exit of Intestines, and Their Suturing
The hernia that appears on the abdomen may be large, medium, or small. The large one can cause a single sac or multiple sacs to protrude, making their insertion and suturing more difficult and challenging.
The small hernia may also be difficult in another way, as its reduction is more challenging. If the sac does not promptly return to its original position within an hour, it swells and thickens, making it difficult to insert. Therefore, the medium-sized hernia is considered best because reducing the sac with it is not as difficult as with these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen can be done in four ways, with two of them being general methods suitable for suturing the abdomen and other bodily wounds, while the other two are specific to suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe each one for you with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and a part of the sac has protruded and its reduction is difficult, this could be due to two reasons: either because the hernia is small, as we said, or because the sac was inflated due to cold air.
If it is so, then you should warm it by immersing a sponge or a damp cloth in hot water alone, or in which he has boiled sedge and saffron and ginger and hyssop until the swelling dissolves.
And this can be done with a drink that contains cucumber, which is better than water alone for dissolving the swelling. So if you see the swelling has dissolved, then rinse with hot water in which you have boiled chamomile or hops, as it will make its entry easier.
If returning after this treatment is difficult, then make a slight incision in the skin with the instrument used for cutting veins and arteries. The first treatment, if possible, is better than making an incision; however, one may be forced to make an incision when necessary and other methods have failed. This is what the instrument looks like:
Its single curved edge is limited, while its other edge is not. The thin end should not be sharp but slightly rounded. It resembles a small lance as you see. If the wound widens and water enters it, then its return should be in its natural shape.
"As for its specific location, if possible, it is best. However, if the wound is extensive and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his leg higher than his head. If it is in the upper abdomen, he should raise his head and chest above his lower body.
"Similarly, if the wound is on one of the two sides of the abdomen, always make the side with the wound higher than the other side. This should be done in extensive wounds and in moderate wounds.
"As for small wounds, place the patient as you see fit, then have a helper who is your equal in skill hold the wound with both hands and close its edges together, then uncover it so that the suturing may proceed step by step.
"This is the common method of suturing, which involves taking one or more needles according to the size of the wound, leaving a length from the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of the bone, and threading one needle through both edges of the skin without the thread entering the wound. "Then, thread the two ends of the suture through both sides of the skin and the subcutaneous tissue until they emerge on the other side, which will bring together the edges of the skin and the subcutaneous tissue, making four layers in total. Then, tie a double knot around the needle from both sides simultaneously until the wound edges are firmly united.
Then leave a suture equal in thickness to the finger and make another stitch, then knot it with the thread as you did with the first stitch. Continue doing this until all of the wound is sutured, and the thread should be moderately thick between thinness and thickness.
Because very thin threads quickly cut the flesh, and thick threads are also difficult to enter the skin, it is advisable for the thread to be moderate between thinness and thickness. It is not appropriate to make a stitch near the edge of the skin.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound opens before healing, and also do not make the suturing too tight so that the wound does not resist healing. Then cut the ends of the thread so that the patient is not disturbed by them during sleep, and make for him bandages from torn cloth on each side holding the ends of the thread.
Leave it until you know that the wound has healed, and this type of stitching with thread is suitable for small wounds because it may be enough to suture them with one or two threads or so.
Another method of suturing is to gather the four lips, meaning the two skin lips and the two muscle lips, in one stitch with a thread that has an adequate length and thickness. Then, if you have secured these four lips with this thread, repeat the thread from the side where you started it.
The thread is interwoven from the top of the wound so that the stitching resembles the stitching used for the bag which the beggar uses, and it makes a small knot between each stitch and the next, and this stitching is known to all people.
By this method, a wound that appeared in the belly of a man who was cut by a sword and whose wound split open more than a finger's width, and he discharged from his intestines about two fingers from the middle intestine, and the splitting occurred in the middle of the belly.
After holding his intestines out of the wound for twenty-four hours, the wound healed within fifteen days, and I treated him until he was cured and lived many years after that, able to function in all aspects of life. The physicians ruled that he could not be cured by any other means.
And it is amazing that I did not treat him with mercury, because I was in a place where there were no medicines. So I used to put clean cotton on the wound twice a day and I made sure to wash it with honey water until he was cured.
As for one of the types of special stitching, I remember it based on Galen's statement, which is to start the stitch from the skin and to pass the needle from outside to inside, so that if the thread penetrates the skin and the abdominal muscles that run vertically along the entire length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the stitch in the side into which I inserted the thread and passed the thread through the other edge from inside to outside on the other edge of the wound, so if I passed it, then I passed it a second time in this edge itself from the wound from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the stitch that is in this side, and pass the thread through its other edge from inside to outside, and when you pass it into the wound, pass it also until it reaches all the edges of the wound.
Then start also from this side, and make its line with the edge that is from the stitch in the other side, and bring out the thread from the skin that is near it, then return it into that skin, and make the line of the edge of the stitch that is in the other side with this edge of the wound.
And bring it out from the skin that is on its side, and do this again and repeat it until you stitch the entire wound in this manner. The characteristic of this stitching is that it resembles the stitching of the linen for linen.
That is to stitch the stitch one time from one side with the edge of the skin and leave it once from the other side until it is free, and as for the second type of special stitch that he also mentioned, this is his statement verbatim.
It is to suture it according to how people among the healers do by gathering each part towards its counterpart, for that has a natural affinity. They bring the edge of the wound to the other edge and the edge of the defect to the other edge, as I have explained to you.
It is necessary to make a stitch in the edge of the defect closest to you from outside and pass it inside only with that, and leave both edges of the wound. Then return the thread and pass it from outside to inside in both edges of the wound together, then return it also.
And pass it from inside to outside in the edge of the other defect that is on the opposite side, and this stitching method is better than ordinary easy sewing, which is the sewing I mentioned before. This is what Galen said, and some have said that abdominal suturing can be done with two threads. And that is because one thread enters both of them, and you begin by inserting the thread from your side and passing it to the other side, and the other thread enters from the opposite side to it, then pass it from your side according to the suturing of the arteries, equally.
You should know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then sewing it up is more difficult than in any other part of the abdomen. As for the treatment of wounds, its rule is the same as for all other wounds, and this is because if you encounter a wound fresh with blood before the air changes it and the humors return to it and regulate it, then carry on it the bandage that is wetted with honey.
If the wound has been altered by the air, then apply some of the herbs that are available to you until it festers and the threads fall out and the skin and flesh adhere together, then treat it like any other wound until it heals.
If you do not have any medicines, then apply cotton wool from the start of festering, and change it twice a day as I have instructed you until it heals, for in most cases you will not need another treatment if the wounds are simple. But if you fear that the wound affects the main parts with pain.
It is necessary to soak soft wool in warm, moderate oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the navel and the pubis. If he feels pain or inflammation in his abdomen, this usually indicates that, so wash him with a black, tight-fitting garment.
And especially if the fistula has reached the bladder and become an open wound into it, know that what is thick of the bladders is easier to cure, and what is thin of them is harder to cure.
And as for the bladder known as the small intestine, it does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it due to the abundance of vessels and bones within it, the delicacy of its tissue, and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. But if what emerges from the wound is urine and it reaches it, return it back to the bladder as you would with the bladder itself.
And if it has been a long time and it has turned green or black, it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the place that has turned black, so that blood discharge does not appear, for there are vessels and veins in the urine. Then cut what is below that tie.
And you make the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound outside of it to facilitate its tightening and removal when urination occurs and the wound secretes, referring to the wound that appears in the bladder. But if a hole appears in the bladder and it is small.
The man who has been pierced by a spear in the abdomen on the right side of the stomach, if the wound is cauterized and becomes swollen with gas and air coming out, I treat him knowing that he will not recover. I continued to care for him until he recovered and the place healed, but when I saw the place heal, I feared a bad accident in his abdomen for the patient, and no such bad event occurred, and he remained in good condition. Indeed, he eats and drinks, copulates, enters the bathhouse, and serves, and some experimentalists have mentioned that if a small wound appears in the bladder, it should be sewn up like this.
This is done by taking the large ants and bringing their heads together, then placing one ant on the edges of the wound with its mouth open. If it closes its mouth and clenches, its head will be cut off and will stick without dissolving. Then another ant is placed near the first one.
And this is done with several ants, according to the size of the wound, then turning it over and sewing the wound, for those heads remain attached in the bladder until it heals and the patient recovers without experiencing any pain. The bladder can also be sewn with fine thread.
The one who is pierced by a poisonous animal attached to him after it enters the wound should have the end of this thread taken from the animal and washed thoroughly, then tied at its end a thin linen thread, which is then inserted into the wound. And in it is the thread of the animal, which he will sew with it and then return to the hollow. This blow from the embroidery with ants and animals is only on the path of greed and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin membranes, there is no remedy for it or a way to escape from it.
Chapter Sixty-Eight.
In the Treatment of Conjunctivitis and Trachoma.
Know that any wound or ulcer, if it becomes chronic and does not heal and continuously produces pus without ceasing, is called, in general, in any limb, trachoma. And we call it conjunctivitis and trachoma.
It is, in fact, a hardened and calcified white bone without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a "feather." And sometimes it is wet, continually producing pus. Sometimes the moisture may stop in certain times. This moisture can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
And it was mentioned in the division when discussing wounds that every wound does not heal and meat does not grow within it, except for one of nine causes. One of them is either due to a lack of blood in the body or its coldness, or because there is hardened flesh inside it and on its edges, which prevents good meat growth. Or because it is excessive in putrefaction and corruption, or because the wound itself is rotten and the nourishing substance that feeds it is of poor quality, or because the medication is not suitable for treating it, or because there has been an epidemic of plague in the city, or because there is something peculiar to the place.
Or because it is excessive in putrefaction and corruption, or because the wound itself is rotten and the nourishing substance that feeds it is of poor quality, or because the medication is not suitable for treating it, or because there has been an epidemic of plague in the city, or because there is something peculiar to the place. As was the case in the city of Syracuse, where diseases are difficult to heal and wounds take a long time to recover by nature, or because it has one bone or several bones. All these causes, their symptoms, and treatments with medications have been explained in the division.
I must now inform you of the treatment for abscesses and pustules, which is done by hand. Know that abscesses can occur in all parts of the body; some end in large veins or nerves, or in nerves.
To the clitoris, or to the bladder, or to the navel, or to one of the ribs, or to a lumbar vertebra from the lumbar vertebrae, or to a joint from the composite joints such as the hand and foot; some extend towards the head and so on.
But any of these veins that have this quality are difficult diseases that do not accept treatment and preoccupation with them is a burden and ignorance, so if there is a vein that has not reached these locations that I mentioned and hoped for treatment for it.
I wanted to reach that knowledge, so take a thin rod of copper or iron if the varicose vein is on an erect (limb), then examine it with it; and if there is swelling in the varicose vein, examine it with a thin rod of lead because lead softens its body.
If it is soft upon entering and tends towards swelling, if the varicose vein has many openings that cannot be reached by the rod, then empty one of its openings with water, for the moisture you fill it with will flow to the other openings and drain.
Do not remove any part of the body that may contain a bone, nerve, or sinew. Also, consider if the tumor is deep-seated or shallow, or if it has many openings and examine all this to the best of your ability when inquiring with the patient about their pain upon touching the area.
And other indications and causative factors of worms are as follows: If you have considered all this truthfully, then proceed to treatment with confidence by examining the patient.
If the tumor appears near to a healthy area, far from a joint, nerve, vessel, or any of the places I mentioned to you, cut open the tumor according to what has been described and remove its soft tissue and rotten flesh.
If it grows in the mouth as meat or a gland and similar things, treat it until it heals. But if the tumor is deep-seated and stable, you should cut it deeply to the extent possible, then clean all its rotten flesh.
Then use the cooked pulp in strong medicines and apply it to the tumor's cavity that you have reached with the iron. Repeat this until the strong medicine eats away all the remaining rot in the tumor's cavity.
Then compel him with the cauteries that promote healthy flesh growth until he recovers. If he does not recover, then burn him as described. If the cause of the fistula is bone and you have confirmed this, then examine and split it as I have informed you, unless you are prevented by an obstacle.
Whether it is a vein, nerve, or limb as we said, if the bone is exposed and has some decay and blackness, then scrape it until the decay disappears. Then treat it with what promotes healing until he recovers. If he does not recover and the fistula persists as before.
Know that you have not reached all the places of decay, so expose everything again and scrape and clean it with all your effort. Then compel it. If he recovers, otherwise know that this decay is deep in a distant cavity, so expose everything again.
Scrape and clean it with all your effort. If the decayed bone is a small bone and can be extracted, then extract it using fine forceps suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, then scrape and extract all of them without leaving any of them behind. Your effort should not be hindered by anything attached to them unless cutting it poses a risk. Then cut those attachments along with the flesh if present and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bone at that time, place on the wound something that will soften it and leave it for several days until what is around those bones of flesh and ligaments softens, and keep the wound so that the split that you have made during your work does not heal.
But if you fear that, place on it a cloth soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian myrrh or any of the green myrrhs, for when those ligaments soften and the bones are freed, their removal is easy and they can be pulled out.
If the bone is large, like the shinbone or thighbone and similar, and only its end has decayed, scrape it vigorously until that darkness and decay disappear; then suture the wound. But if the decay reaches a significant part of the bone and extends to the marrow, it must be excised.
Cut it all the way to where the decay ends, and then treat it until it heals. I will tell you about a man who had a boil on his leg as an example to help you in your treatment. This man was elderly.
"Thirty years ago, he experienced pain in his leg due to a movement inside the body until the material reached the leg and swelled with a large worm, and there was no external cause. Then time passed with the doctors' mistakes until the worm burst.
"Many materials exited him, which was poorly treated until the leg swelled and had many openings, all of them discharging pus and body fluids. A group of doctors treated him for about two years, none of whom were skilled in manual craft.
"Until they brought him to me, and I saw his leg with material pouring from those openings in great amounts, and the man's body had wasted away, and his complexion had turned yellow. So I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe went to the bone, then all the openings began to drain.
"And I found it spreading in all directions throughout the leg, so I hurried and cut into one of those openings until I exposed part of the bone, and I found it rotten and eaten away by blackness and decay, with holes that extended to the marrow, which then spread what was revealed to me.
I treated the infected bone, and I believe that there is no infection in the bone other than what I cut out and removed. I bandaged it and then forced the wound to heal with medicated dressings for a long time, but it did not heal. Then I reopened it again above the first opening.
I found that the infection was attached to the bone, so I cut out whatever appeared to me of that infection and then forced the wound to heal, but it did not heal nor did it close up. So I opened it again, but I continued cutting the bone piece by piece and forcing it to heal until I had almost nothing left of the bone.
I removed it with a spoon, then forced it to heal with medicines, and it healed quickly and completely. This repetition in his condition was due to the weakness of the patient, his low resistance, and my fear that he would die because he had profuse diarrhea at all times.
In place of the bone, flesh grew solid and his condition in his body improved, his strength returned, and he resumed his normal activities without any impediment in walking. If the bone had broken in a part of the body, it would have been a severe injury that would permanently disable him.
Take a bandage, tighten it at the end of the broken bone, and command whoever is assisting to raise it upward; then take another bandage of wool thicker than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh below the bone and extend its end so that the flesh is pulled downward, while you expose the flesh from the place you want to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh; then place a small piece of wood or a board firmly under the bone from below.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the rotten bone, and it is necessary that the sawing be a little above the place of the rottenness so that there is no rottenness in the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its surface to the touch, and thus you are forced to saw again.
If the bone is rotten and not broken, but some parts are connected and the rottenness is in the middle or in a part of it, then expose all the flesh from every side; then place the wood from below and saw it from the first side where the rottenness is.
Then when you have finished sawing it from one side, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the rottenness as we said. If the rottenness is in a joint, cut the rotten joint itself, and saw the bone where it connects with the other side.
If the decay is between two joints, there is no remedy other than cauterization. If the decay is in the palm of the hand or the sole of the foot, its treatment is very difficult, but it is necessary to scrape off the decay however it appears to you and cauterize it and cleanse it in any way possible.
And whatever method is suitable for you when you are not faced with a vein or nerve, and know that the saws and knives for cutting these bones are many according to the position of the bones, their arrangement, hardness, softness, size, and density. Therefore, it is necessary to have a suitable tool for each type of work.
And know that the works themselves may indicate to you the type of tool needed if you have a long drill and knowledge of these arts, for from this craft comes the skillful artisan who has witnessed various diseases. He may therefore devise for himself the tools that suit him for each disease, and I will depict for you in the remainder of this chapter several tools.
You make them examples to be marveled at, and a measure by which others are measured if God wills. Image of a saw:
