"Chapter Sixty-Nine.
"On Cupping and How to Use It.
"Cupping glasses can be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass. The cupping glass has two types: one is called the bloodletting cupping with a hole for releasing blood, and the other is called dry cupping without a hole.
"And this type of cupping that does not have a hole also comes in two forms.
"And the cupping glasses for the neck are two places on both sides of the neck, and the cupping glasses for the chin are under the lower jaw from the mouth, and the cupping glasses for the shoulders, and the cupping glasses for the temples on the back of the head, and the cupping glasses for the elbows in the middle of the arms, and the cupping glasses for the calves." ‌
"And the bloodletting, it only draws blood from the thin, soft veins in the flesh, and for this reason, do not apply it to expel pus, nor should bloodletting be used with fire or without fire in any of the diseases that cause swelling until the body is completely cleansed.
"So if the need for bloodletting arises from a disease or habit, we use it at any time in the first half of the month, in the middle, and at any time.
"And this is because some people, if their blood increases to the point where they need to have it removed by bloodletting, find heaviness and dizziness in their head; among them are those who find swelling and redness in their face, head, and neck; among them are those who find wrinkles on their face, cheeks, and darkness and black spots under their eyes; and among them are those who feel the place of their veins.
"And among them are those whose laughter increases; and among them are those who taste blood in their mouth, who have a dry throat, and whose blood spurts; and among them are those whose sleep increases; and among them are those who see blood, redness, murders, wounds, and things similar to that in their sleep. Whenever we see any of this.
And specifically, if it is in the middle third of the month, we order bleeding after two or three hours have passed from noon. As for the benefit of cephalic bleeding, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water that flows to the eyes.
But it is fitting for this to be done after the body has been emptied of its contents, and this bleeding may serve as a substitute for phlebotomy. It should not be used by those who have cold brains or headaches, as it will cause them great harm.
Therefore, it is not advisable for the elderly and those with cold diseases of the head to use it, nor should those who are accustomed to it become pregnant, as this will cause forgetfulness in them.
Therefore, it is advisable that the bleeder be instructed to lower his hand with the bleeding cup a little towards the bottom, fearing the onset of forgetfulness. As for calf bleeding, it serves as a substitute for phlebotomy and saphenous vein phlebotomy.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, wheezing, and chest fullness and congestion. It is advisable to raise the calf bleeding slightly because if it descends too low, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
The drawing of blood from the veins is beneficial for headaches, vertigo, toothache, and pain in the roots of the teeth, as a substitute for cupping. It is necessary to instruct the blood-letter not to insert his hand deeply with the needle lest he cut a vessel and cause hemorrhage.
As for phlebotomy at the wrist, it is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, decay of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth, and can replace cupping on the cheeks. Phlebotomy from the shoulders is beneficial for heat-induced delirium resulting from excessive drinking or fever.
As for phlebotomy at the ankles, it is beneficial for whatever cupping of the three large veins (basilic, cephalic, and median cubital) is beneficial for, such as bloodletting, hematoma, and pleurisy. This is because it draws blood from all those thin veins in the flesh and draws those thin veins from other veins thicker than them until the draw reaches the three thick veins.
It is necessary to instruct the blood-letter not to press with the needle, because the area is bare of flesh and below it are nerves and vessels. As for single phlebotomy that draws from the veins, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and anal fissures.
It is necessary to command the cuppers that the cup be large and made of copper, for the place requires a powerful suction, and perhaps a glass cup may break, which has many conditions attached. As for the cups for legs, they lack fullness by half.
Because it draws blood from all parts of the body and benefits chronic pains in the kidneys, liver, bladder, and expels urine, and benefits hemorrhoids and nosebleeds, and establishes the position of the spleen and kidneys, except that it weakens the body greatly and causes coldness in most people. The benefit of cups for the spleen is close to the benefit of cups for the legs.
The method of applying the cups is to first place the cup empty and apply moderate suction, not prolonging the application of the cups, but placing them quickly and removing them quickly so that the blood flows into the area evenly. You continue this until you see the area turn red and open up.
When the redness of the blood appears, then it is necessary to apply suction gently and repeatedly, then observe the state of the bodies. If a person has thin flesh with wide pores, it is necessary to suction them once only, lest the place become inflamed.
The barber is commanded to widen the wound slightly and adjust the suture in movement and gentle motion, for if the blood is thick, it is necessary to suture twice: first, to open a path for the thin blood and its water, and second, to ensure the removal of the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, the suture is repeated a third time to achieve fullness. In summary, if we want to remove little blood, one suture will suffice; but if we want to remove much blood, we suture more, especially if we judge the blood to be thick.
It is necessary to suture with a deep stitch, and the average depth of the stitch should be just the skin. The following are advised when applying ligatures: Avoid using ointments, water, or anything that might irritate the wound, especially if the skin is thick, hard, calloused, and the pores are small.
It is necessary to anoint the sites of the ligatures with soft, thin, and mild ointments. If it is summertime, use ointments such as alkheira oil, benzoin oil, sweet almond oil, or henna seed oil.
And as for if it is in winter, then like the oil of narcissus or the oil of cypress or the oil of peony or the oil of anemone and its likes, so if the advantage is thick and cold, let the oil be oil of almonds or oil of pine nuts or oil of sesame or oil of linseed and its likes.
And if the patient has wide wounds, it is necessary to prevent the oil from entering them. These patients should wash their wounds after cupping with rose water or cold water or water of wild grapes or water of myrtle or water of iris and its likes; but if their blood is very moist.
...their wounds should be washed with vinegar or water of ashes and sumac and its likes; but if their humors are thick, they should wash their wounds with old wine or water of almonds or linseed decoction or peony and its likes. And it is necessary to avoid cupping in the bath and after the bath.
Rather, it is necessary to use cupping an hour or two after leaving the bath, and no one should sleep after cupping, for whatever is harmful to the patient and the cupper before and after cupping.
You should first observe whether the patient is anemic or pale, and if bleeding and inflammation predominate. In such a case, he should take cooling foods like pomegranates, apricots with vinegar, cucumbers, eggplants, lemons, and similar foods, and his diet should include fried meat of the turtledove and similar dishes.
And if his temperament is cold, it is necessary to drink honey drink or the syrup of unripe eggplants, and to eat moderately scented wine that is between old and new, and he should be ordered to limit food, and his diet should include fried partridges, quails, thrushes, and pigeon breast. It is necessary for the drink to be more on the day of bleeding and purging than food.
And it may be necessary for some people to drink in some cases a certain infusion or musk medicine or shilajit before bleeding and purging, or after it, to strengthen the main organs and thicken the blood, but those who are prone to bleeding should not drink it.
As for bleeding that is without a bandage, it is the type placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, pelvis, and the kidney area, because these organs do not tolerate being bandaged. This method aims to draw blood from one part of the body to another.
We place the cupping glass on the breasts during fever or use it to extract a cold odor from the limb in which there is pain, just as we place the cupping glass on the abdomen and chest, for it warms the limb and removes the pain by releasing that odor.
It can also be placed on the kidneys if they have swelling or stones, for its drawing power may open the blockage or remove the stone from its place. Similarly, it acts in this way when placed on the liver and spleen if there is a bad odor in them. These cupping glasses are sometimes used empty except for their seal.
It can also be used with fire, or filled with hot water in the case of swelling, by filling the cupping glass with boiling water alone or with water in which some suitable herbs have been cooked.
Then it is placed full on the area and secured, then removed and repeated as needed. This is the shape of the cupping glass used with fire:
Its mouth will be two open fingers wide as we have illustrated and its depth will be half a finger. On one of its sides, halfway up, there will be a small hole just large enough for the needle to enter. This needle is made of thick Chinese copper or yellow copper.
A flat, smooth pad so that the member is not injured when placed on it, with a hole in its center where the wax candle is inserted into the fire, and this bandage can be made larger or smaller depending on the disease.
And we use it for children and women, not for men or pregnant women, because the way to place this bandage in the fire on the member is to ignite a cloth or small wax candle from cotton, and place it on the center of the hard penis that is in the middle of the bandage.
The fire should rise to the bottom of the bandage so that the patient's body does not burn, then place it on the member and put your finger in the hole we mentioned until when we hold the bandage, we do not need to remove the finger.
The steam will come out from that hole and the bandage will soften in its place, then ignite the cloth again and repeat it if needed. As for the bandage used for the disease of scabies with water, there is no hard penis or hole in it.
It is only used by filling it with water and placing it on the limb alone, and this is its shape.
The more capacious the bag, the better it is for holding a lot of water.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On Ligation of Blood Vessels.
The blood vessel is used in most cases for the limbs on which the ligature cannot be placed, either because of their small size, such as the lip and the gum, or because the limb is devoid of flesh, such as the finger and the nose.
Its use is such that you should take from the blood vessel that is in clean, pure water, then leave it for a day and night in fresh water until it becomes dry and nothing remains inside it. Then the body is first purged with purgatives or by bloodletting, and then the affected limb is washed.
Until it becomes red; then the ligature is placed on it. If it swells and falls off, and if it is possible to touch the place with the bag, this is best for benefit. Otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with a lot of water, and squeeze it, for if the blood flow persists after the ligature falls off, this was oozing; so dip a piece of cotton in cold water.  />
He will place it on top until the flow stops; if there is excessive bleeding, he will sprinkle powdered alum or eggshells or similar substances from the cups until the bleeding stops, or half a peeled pomegranate will be placed on the spot.
Let it sit until the pomegranate adheres to the spot, for then the bleeding will stop. If another binding is needed, do not use that same bandage if possible; rather, if the bandage resists being tied again, wash the spot with fresh blood.
Or a needle may be inserted into the spot until it brings out some blood; if this is done, it should be left in place. If something of the blood adheres to the spot, it will fall off by itself. But if you wish to hasten its falling off, sprinkle on it a little sumac or salt or ashes, for it will then fall off at once.
Chapter Three.
In the section on fractures.
This chapter also includes what is needed in the practice of medicine, namely, setting broken and dislocated bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter may be derided by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, as well as those who have not previously delved into the books of the ancients or read a word from them.
This art has become obscure in our country, and I have never encountered anyone proficient in it. I only learned from him what I have learned throughout my reading of the earliest books and by striving to understand them, until I extracted that knowledge from them. Then I devoted myself to practice and experimentation throughout my life.
I have illustrated for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. My experience confirms this after I have brought it close to you, liberated it from the branches of prolixity, and summarized it to the utmost brevity; I have also made its meaning as clear as possible and provided you with many illustrations of the instruments used in it.
For this is an increase in explanation, as I have done in the preceding chapters, and there is no power but with Allah, the Exalted, the Great.
The First Section.
Before we begin describing the broken and dislocated bones, it is necessary to mention in the beginning of this chapter some general statements and sections. First, you must understand and comprehend them, as well as those who are eager to learn this noble art besides you.
I say, whenever it happens to anyone with a fracture, dislocation, or fall, it is necessary to hasten first to evacuate or discharge, or both. If the obstacle to doing so is weakness of strength, or if what happened was a child or an old man with hernia, or if it was very hot or extremely cold weather.
Then, he should restrict his food to cold porridge, bird meat, and grapes, and avoid drinking, fatty meats, overeating, and any food that causes blood flow until the worm is safe and there is no possibility of the mass moving to its place.
At that time, the patient should return to his original diet which he was used to. If the broken bone is in the stomach, then it is necessary for the patient to eat foods that are abundant, fatty, and substantial, such as herrings, rice, raisins, dried figs, and sheep's trotters. And eggs, dried fish, and thick drinks like this. With this diet, the bone will heal faster and better. Know that if the broken bones are in strong men or old people, they will never reunite and heal according to their original nature.
The bones and their density, and some bones may adhere and fuse together to such an extent that they become as soft as the bones of young children. However, nature grows on the fractured bone from all sides something resembling a hard shell that sticks to it and tightens it until parts of it become interlocked with each other.
And it connects parts together until it reaches the utmost strength and stability as it was initially, so that nothing hinders its functions.
Therefore, it is necessary to provide the patient with food that contains strength, softness, and hardness, as we have mentioned. And know that fractures vary according to the differences in limbs, for a fracture of the shin bone differs from a fracture of the skull, and a fracture of the ribs differs from a fracture of the spine.
Similarly, all the limbs differ from one another, and we will discuss each type of fracture in detail within its own chapter, distinguishing it from others. The type of bone fracture can also vary within itself.
Because a fracture can be a clean break without any fragments, or it can be a long bone fracture with or without displaced and undisplaced fragments. The fracture can also occur with a wound or laceration in the skin, or the fracture can be a greenstick fracture.
For each type of fracture, there is a specific method of reduction, which will be mentioned in detail in its place. What is called a bone break: swelling around it, tenderness, sensitivity to touch, and shaking upon touching it with the hand.
When there is no swelling, shaking, or sensitivity in the area when pressing on the bone and the patient does not feel much pain, it is likely that there is no fracture but rather a sprain, a minor fracture, or a wandering hematoma. In this case, it should not be moved by stretching or continuous tapping.
Instead, apply the medicines mentioned later on to the area when it is suitable, then wrap it with a soft bandage, and know that if the bone splinters or crumbles without any swelling occurring, it means that every part has separated from its companion.
It is necessary for you to hurry from your place to its alignment and straightening before it becomes hot with inflammation. If it does become hot with inflammation, leave it for days until the heat of the inflammation subsides, then align it in any way you can manage with gentleness and skill.
"Know that straightening and setting it is easier than the bone in which a fracture has occurred, then it will be difficult to handle what will be mentioned afterwards. If there are splinters in the bone, it is necessary to extend the broken limb from both sides, whether it is a hand or a foot.
"If it is a small limb, do this with your hand; if large, use two cords or a cord and your hand together. Place the limb on a flat surface in its natural shape until the broken bone fragment extends, at which point remove those excess parts from their places with all possible care and skill.
"Strive to ensure that you do not cause the patient pain, discomfort, or injury through your actions. Make sure one bone is aligned with its counterpart in the best possible manner. At this time, it is necessary to touch and feel them both with your hand. If you see anything amiss, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
"Avoid excessive extension and strong pulls, as many of the ignorant do, for they often cause burns or bruising in the limb, as I have seen them do repeatedly.
Then after the alignment, practice, and strengthening of that limb, rest, immobility, and caution are required to prevent the patient from moving it during waking hours or sleep, and during turning and all movements to their full extent, and that he should not attempt for the position of the limb to be one that ensures safety.
And that is because when he feels pain or discomfort in the limb's position, he should move it to a different position where he does not feel pain, and he may prefer for his position to be level and upright.
So that there is no discomfort in the limb if it swells, and as for how to strengthen a broken limb, I will describe it to you: know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and types, so whatever of them is small like the arm, finger, or ankle, etc.
So it is necessary that the bandages be broad and soft for a slender limb, and if it is rough like the thigh, back, or chest, then the bandages should be firm because wide bindings are required to secure the large limb and strengthen it from all sides with even pressure, without any gap.
Then begin after finishing the leveling by carrying the suitable plaster for that. In a soft bandage over the place of the fracture, then begin by wrapping the bandage in the same place of the fracture three or four turns according to what the limb requires, and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then go with it to the upper part of the fracture site.
Slightly less tightly than the fracture site, then separate the turn slightly from the fracture and gradually reduce the tightness a little until you take something right from the correct place, then take another bandage and also wrap it on the fracture site.
Wraps, then go with the turn to the lower part of the fracture, and your action in tightening the turn and wrapping it should be as we mentioned in wrapping the upper first, then place between the layers of soft bandages or cloth what is suitable for the swelling of the fracture.
If there is swelling, place nothing in it; otherwise, do not put anything in it. Then wrap another bandage over it and cover these turns with the firm splints of your garment if the limb has no inflammation or warm wound.
If there is swelling or inflammation, place upon it what will soothe that inflammation and remove the swelling, and leave it for several days; then bind it with the appropriate bandage, and let the bandage be made from half of the stalk of the plant known as 'al-'urad', prepared with skill, or let the bandage be made from the wood of the 'gharbily'.
Which is made from the fig or palm leaves, or aloe, or colocynth, and similar things that are available; and let each bandage be of this form and shape exactly as shown.
Except that the bandage placed on a fracture should be thicker and slightly wider than other bandages, as for the length of the bandage, it is made according to the size of the broken limb, whether large or small. The shape of the bandage is as follows:.
Then, another bandage is bound on top of the previous one in the same manner; then it is tied at the top with strong strings, as mentioned, that the binding should be tighter at the site of the fracture. And each time you move away from the fracture, the binding becomes looser.
And the strings should be of medium thickness and softness, and they should be made of wet cotton, especially because if the strings are thick like what you see the ignorant people doing, making their strings from strips of unraveled cotton, it is a great mistake.
The string is not suitable because it does not reach what you want, and it should not be less than a finger between the knots.
If the patient is hurt by the ends of the strings after being tied in the correct places, place soft cloth or fluffed wool under them so that nothing hurts him from it. And if the fracture is with a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will mention its treatment separately.
Know that not every broken bone should be tied with strings from the first day, and this is because if the bone is large, it is not advisable to place strings on it except after five days or seven or more, depending on how confident you are about the occurrence of inflammation.
This is the nature of the bandages that were used by the ancients for fractures, dislocations, and sprains; a generally useful, concise bandage that fixes the fracture and suits most cases, especially children and women, because it does not overcome cold or heat.
He takes flour dust from the mouth of the mill during the rotation of the millstone, kneads it without straining it with egg whites, making his dough neither thick nor thin, then bakes it.
Another kind of plaster compels fracture, dislocation, and dislocation to heal. It is made from alum, vitriol, aqua fortis, rosemary, verdigris, and mercury, each a hundred dirhams; and from marjoram and patience a hundred dirhams; and from olive oil twenty-two dirhams; and from Armenian or Roman clay twenty dirhams. All the ingredients are ground and mixed with olive oil or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot. Then this plaster is used, for it has a good composition that quickly compels fractured bones to heal and is suitable for most people due to its moderation.
Another plaster that is also beneficial for fractures and dislocations: Take verdigris, alum, and white vitriol, each ten dirhams; marjoram and patience, each five dirhams; aqua fortis six dirhams; Armenian clay twenty dirhams. Grind everything finely, mix well, knead with water or egg white, and use it.
An ointment for joints and displaced bones, which causes a persistent pain in them and in the fracture and break of the bone. Cotton wool is taken, soaked in vinegar and cooked oil, and placed on the spot; this ointment does not have the power of compulsion.
But it is excellent in relieving burning pain and driving away particular pains; another ointment compels the broken bone, taking the leaves of the deaf fig and the wild almond tree, grinding all together and making a paste with them wet; another condensed ointment is used when there is an outpouring of bone fracture.
If you desire to dissolve the remaining pain, take equal parts of chamomile root, wild peony, hemp seed and poppy seed, grind them all together and knead with honey. If the limb is not inflamed, knead it with vinegar water or plain water and use it.
Another stronger ointment for dissolving [pain] is used when hard pain occurs with bone fracture, taking equal parts of chamomile root, hyacinth bean seed, flaxseed, liquorice, hemp seed, and peony root, grinding them all together.
The general practice is to bind and bandage with cold water, fresh water, or lead. All this depends on the heat of the limb and its rest, and as for splints, it was not mentioned in his book that they should be placed on a broken limb when setting it.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil, which is described as being of moderate consistency between thick and thin, Galen recommended that in setting a broken limb, materials containing cavities should be used along with something warm like honey, syrup, or milk, and so on as we have mentioned.
A plaster for warmth and pain relief is beneficial. It is made from the gall of a castrated person or a bird's feather, gum mastic, salt, and water. It should be mixed, kneaded, and bandaged with it, and as for the amount that should remain in the bandage.
Then it is to be loosened, for if there is no pain or crackling and the broken bone does not move from its place, do not remove it after many days. But if it causes severe crackling or intense pain or swelling in that place.
In the text, it is emphasized to begin the treatment promptly in its appropriate time and not delay this matter, as neglecting it would be disastrous. Then, take a soft cloth or a moist marine sponge and soak it in cold water. Use it to wash the affected area until the pain subsides and the discomfort is relieved. Afterward, leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then, place the wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil on it, and secure it with a bandage for the night until the hot worm subsides and the swelling of the limb diminishes and its pain eases. Afterward, return it to a gentle and light bandage, not tightening it as much as before.
Continue treating it until it heals completely. If you observe that the worm, redness, pain, swelling, and all symptoms have disappeared and the limb requires bandaging and tightening again, then bandage it as you did initially. However, if no issues arise in the limb, as mentioned earlier.
Do not bandage it except after three, four, five, or seven days; it may even be left for twenty days, all depending on the condition of the limb as we mentioned earlier, until such time that the fracture demands and approaches the union of the flesh upon it.
He also increased it then in the swelling, more than the first swelling entirely, and he also increased it in irritating the food of the patient as mentioned before. So if you see the place of the fracture has become dry and hard beyond what is appropriate, know that the food is prevented from reaching it.
Wash it with clean water every time you dress it, and do this every three days. Reduce the swelling a little, for by doing so the nourishment will reach the limb and it will heal quickly. As for what the ignorant do by forcefully breaking the limb again.
If he was not properly set in the first place and is forcibly reset to a different position, that is an error on their part and a great delusion. Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, but I have not seen any of them doing so. The correct thing is not to do that.
Chapter Two.
On fractures of the skull.
The types of fractures of the skull are many, their shapes varied, and their causes diverse. Some fractures occur due to a sword strike and may either heal completely or extend to the membrane beneath the bone. As with walking on wood.
And therefore this type of fracture is called a walking fracture. Or if the sword cuts off part of the bone and its surface only, without completing the cut to the other side, then this type of fracture is called a simple avulsion. The wound in these two types of fractures can be either large or small.
Among the fractures, there is one that can be blunt or crushed, and its cause may be a blow with a stone or falling on a stone or something similar. This fracture may either be an open fracture near the periosteum beneath the bone, or it may be in the bone itself.
And the wounds in these two fractures can also be either large or small. Among the fractures, there is one that may be hidden within the bone in the hairline and it is a sliding fracture.
And for this reason, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture. Among these is a fracture that occurs from a fall or the impact of a stone and similar things, where the bone plate enters inside and becomes a depression in that place, just as copper shows when it is struck, part of it enters inside.
And this is most common in the soft bone of the head, such as the crowns of young children. All these types of fractures can have fragments that are detached and not detached, and we will mention their treatment in its place. All these types of fractures are known by the term "exposure.
And examine it with probes, and remove the rotten flesh from it. As for the sinewy type, it is recognized by uncovering the bone and wiping and tapping on it with a hammer, as the fracture appears black. As for treating the fracture, one should first look at the symptoms of the patient.
For if you see from his symptoms any obvious sign of fear, such as cold sweats, trembling, loss of consciousness, mutism, anxiety, severe fever, and the like, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is inevitable with these symptoms in most cases.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his safety, then begin his treatment, which is: If the wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded and this happened during the winter days.
It is necessary to make an effort to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, in any case, and if it is during the summer days, then hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, lest what is under the bone, the covering, becomes infected, leading to those symptoms that we have described.
It is necessary to cut off the damaged and infected part, as I have described to you, by amputating the head of the wounded person.
And expose the bone in any way you can, according to the shape of the wound and what is hidden from the patient. If you see bleeding or hot pus upon exposing the bone, it should be treated accordingly.
And that is to wash the area with vinegar in water and oil, and cleanse the wound until the pus settles and the bleeding stops. Then proceed to reinforce the bone and remove it, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first method, it is to cut the bone with a fine, sharp saw, as shown in this figure.
Then, use another cutter after this one, slightly wider than it, as shown in this figure.
Then use another cutter that is also wider than the previous one, and it should be from a set of various cutters with different widths and lengths, and their edges should be extremely sharp, made of Indian steel or good iron.
To use the saw on the section, so that you do not shake the head and hurt it; if the bone is hard and solid, you should bore around it before using the saws called non-curved saws, but they are called non-curved saws.
It does not exceed the bone of the skull beyond it, because the drill has a circular edge at its tip resembling a small ring or circle, which prevents it from sinking and penetrating the brain.
And you should take many of these saws, each one suitable for the thickness of that bone, until you have for every skull a saw suitable in length and width to the thickness of that skull. This is the image of three types. Of large, medium, and small saws:
As for how to bore around the fractured bone, it is to place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been perforated; then move the drill to another place and make a hole at an interval between each bore equal to the thickness of the marrow or similar.
Then cut with the scissors what is between each of the two holes, and do that as much as you can manage on it, as we said, until the bone breaks, either by hand or by something else from some of the tools I have prepared for that purpose.
Like the scalpel and the blunt scissors, it is necessary to be extremely careful that nothing touches the hole or the cut from the hair, because if the bone is broken and freed from the hair, if it was attached to it, then it should be extracted and skinned.
And smooth the remaining bone with another tool that resembles the scissors, except it should be finer and more delicate than the other scissors, for if there is something left of the small bones and splinters, then remove them gently with what tools you have available.
Then treat the wound after that with lint and bandages as mentioned, and as for the other aspect of the work, it is a straightforward task far from error, as Galen mentioned and praised greatly, saying this: It is necessary for you to first begin by uncovering a part of the bone from the place where it broke until, when you have uncovered that part, you will see underneath it this oval cut and here is its image:
The lens part of it should be soft, not cutting anything, and the sharp part of it on its sides that go along the length as you see, so that the lens part is supported by the eyelid, and the face of the cutter is sharp on the bone, then strike the cutter from one side.
With a small hammer, until all the bone is cut completely as it turns, while you are safe from the covering, nothing bad happens to you. Even if the surgeon does not know the people and deceives them, yes, even if he was stupid.
If there is still something of the bone cover left in some places of the bone, scrape it off with the same lens part of the cutter and remove it completely by turning. It removes it without harm or fear.
As for if the bone fracture does not extend to the covering, and what was cut from the bone is its face, and there remains in it softness and spicules of bone, then it is necessary to scrape off that softness and remove those spicules with a bone rasp of various sizes, so that you can use them in every place according to its need and repair it as the work requires.
The bone and its shape, and it is advisable to use in your sawing first a wider blade for the bone, then use after it one thinner than it, and do not stop doing this until you reach using the thinnest and most precise of them all.
As for the rest of the small hairline cracks and fine fractures, it is advisable to use in each a treatment suitable for its healing, which is not hidden from those who have any knowledge in this craft, according to what we have written and clarified regarding large fractures.
If the covering remains exposed after cutting the bone, it is advisable to take a cloth of cotton according to the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another piece, either double or triple-layered. Then soak it in a drink and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, doing this with as little weight as possible to avoid making the bandage heavy; then use over it a wide bandage and tighten it only enough to hold the wound in place.
Keep it with this treatment for a day or two until the inflammation subsides; then remove it and use after that the medicines which have beneficial dryness, such as the roots of sows, wheat flour, and ground cardamom, and similar things. This is done by making pastes from these medicines.
It is preferable to leave the wound dry and as a whole, so use for it a medicine that heals without irritating. And be careful in your effort to treat the wound that it may be clean, not containing oil or pus, and do not let the hair gather in it, where dirt accumulates.
Because the hair, if it gathers on the surface of the brain, corrupts and spoils it, which causes the patient great harm. It may also appear on the top of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if neglected in treatment.
If blackness appears on its surface, and the patient shows the symptoms we mentioned, know that death is inevitable. Even if the blackness is caused by a medicine applied to it, and this medicine has such potency to cause this blackness.
Take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, and soak a bandage in it. Then place the bandage on the wound and treat it with various methods until it heals.
Chapter Three.
If the nose is broken, it must be set.
You should know that from the nose only the upper jawbones fracture entirely or one of them, because they are bones, while the lower part is cartilage and does not fracture; rather, it swells and bruises.
If one of its jaws is fractured, it is necessary for the little finger to enter through the nostril and straighten that break from inside with your index finger and thumb from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Let this be done gently so that no pain occurs to the patient due to you.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, it must be straightened with a blunt object a little thicker than the finger. If the fracture is in both directions, do the same. And he should begin to treat it on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the inflammation has subsided, then enter a linen thread into the nostril if the fracture is in one direction, or two threads if the fracture is in both directions.
And let the thread be thick enough to fill the nostril opening. Some early physicians mentioned that the thread should be greased with fat and changed every day, but I do not see this as necessary. Rather, the thread should be dipped in egg white mixed with flour dust.
Then leave the tampon until the bone is solidified and the swellings have subsided, and you can insert into the nose tubes of wool threads after they are softened by boiling, so that their restraint for a nasal fracture is stronger and lest the patient finds it difficult to breathe.
And this is not necessary, if you wish you can make it or you can make tampons. If the nose swells during your work due to a hot iron, then narrow the nose with a cotton swab soaked in vinegar and rose oil or something from the ointments of Dioscorides.
If it does not swell from a hot iron, then it is necessary to narrow it from outside with finely ground flour and candied sugar mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage on it, and do not bind the nose with anything tight, for if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it shatters, then it is necessary to drill into it and remove it with the appropriate instrument, then suture the hole and treat it with what binds and adheres from the ointments suitable for that, for if there is an internal injury in the nose, it should be treated with a tampon and silver tubes should be used. Until he is healed.
Chapter Four
If the lower jaw is broken.
If the lower jaw is broken without a wound, and if it breaks only from the outside and does not break in two places, and splinters into pieces inside, its diagnosis is easy.
If the break is on the right side, the thumb of the left hand should enter the patient's mouth and do likewise if the break is on the left side, using the thumb of the right hand to raise the fragment from within outwards.
The other hand, placed outside the bone, controls its alignment. If the jaw fracture has broken into two parts, it is necessary to use a splint from both sides for stability until alignment is achieved. If it occurs in the teeth, it may cause shifting.
Or, after splinting, fasten it with gold or silver wire, then place a conical cap on the broken jaw, followed by a double bandage. Place a large, tight weight or a piece of leather boot sole equal in length to the jaw over the bandage. Then connect it from above according to what you prepare for its connection, ensuring it matches the suture until it does not break, and order the patient to rest and be calm, and make his food soft and light. If you think that something has changed in the shape by one of the ways, then hasten to open it on the third day.
Then connect it from above according to what you prepare for its connection, ensuring it matches the suture until it does not break, and order the patient to rest and be calm, and make his food soft and light. If you think that something has changed in the shape by one of the ways, then hasten to open it on the third day. Then mend that which you have sutured with flour mixed with egg white or fine semolina after removing the splint from it, and place on the wound a light bandage. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the bone's condition does not change, leave it alone.
Do not open it until it heals and the fracture strengthens, for this fracture often takes three months to heal. If there is heat in the wound during that time, then use what we have mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until the heat subsides.
As for if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, and if you see that the bone has shattered into pieces or fragments, then be gentle in removing those fragments with whatever tool is suitable for their removal. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, then widen it with the scalpel to the extent necessary.
Then, if those fibers are removed and nothing remains of them, cut the wound in the mouth if it is wide; otherwise, place one of the suitable bandages on it. And let the wound heal until it clears up.
Chapter Five
Set the fracture if it is broken.
The bone most commonly breaks in front of the elbow, at the end of the humerus, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and fragments into two pieces without displacement, which is the easiest to set.
And if displacement occurs in the fracture, it is the most difficult to set; or if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, the procedure is as follows: If the fracture is without a wound, two assistants are needed—one to immobilize the limb containing the broken bone while the other extends the neck towards the opposite side.
Then, straighten the fracture with your fingers until it achieves the correct shape without any bend or distortion. If more extension is needed, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's elbow.
And the bone will be as much as you need, and extend and raise the arm, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is straightened as needed. If you cannot pull the end of the bone out because it has gone deep, the patient should lie on their side.
توضع تحت منكبه مخدة متوسطة في العظم، ويكبس الخادم منكبه إلى أسفل حتى يرتفع عظم الترقوة الذي في العمق إلى فوق. فعندئذ فأصلح الكسر وسواه بأصابعك.
فإن أحسست أنّه قد انكسرت شظية من الترقوة وصارت تتحرّك، فإنبغى أن تشقّ عليها وتخرج تلك الشظية برفق. فإن كانت الشظية محتبسة في العظم، فاستخدم أحد المقاطع التي أعددتها لذلك. بعد أن تصير تحت الترقوة الآلة التي تحفظ الصفاق، وهي آلة من خشب أو من حديد، وهذه صورتها:
ملعقة ليس لها تعقير، ويكون عرضها على حسب ما تحتاجه من كبر العظم أو صغره. وأمّا طولها فعلى حسب ما يمكنك العمل به، ولتكون ذات طرفين: كما ترى الطرف الواحد أوسع والآخر أضيق.
If the cut of the wound which you have sewn near the bone exit is wide and the hot worm has taken refuge, sew the lips of the wound with stitches, and if the cut is narrow or you fear the hot worm, cover the wound with wool and bandages to the extent that it cuts into the wound.
If there is a hot worm, soak the cut in rose oil and vinegar or wine and carry it on; as for tightening of the bone if it is not from a wound or a cut, then use a poultice made of flour from the mill with egg white. Place the soft splint on it, then place a ball under the bandage if needed; then take a long strip, its width being about a handbreadth or similar, and place two layers of wool on the splint and poultice, then take a sheet from a thin board. Its width is three handbreadths, and in length likewise; then insert it into a cut (slit), then insert that cut with the board into the place of the strip on the fracture site, then tighten the strip over the fracture as you turn it. And the tourniquet on his neck, and beneath the corrective bandage, and beneath the patient's bandage, and it was wrapped around all sides. As you saw, the tightness controlled the fracture tightly, which is clear to you.
And all this is so that the fat does not melt away from the broken bone. And therefore, the patient should be checked every day to see if the bandage has loosened and the fat has melted away. If so, correct this by tightening the bandage, then make the patient lie on his back during sleep, and place a small cushion under the bandage at night.
So that his limb is raised off the ground, raising the fracture along with it by raising the humerus or tie his arm to his neck. Do not unbind the bandage unless there is an event of inflammation or worms after twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see a need for it. And wrap it tightly and leave it until the humerus heals and the fracture consolidates, and it becomes stronger and more stable, especially in twenty-eight days. And some people may heal in less time than that.
Chapter Six
When the shoulder is broken in its wide part, only its head breaks. But when it is broken in any other place or if the break occurs in the middle, this is called a displaced fracture.
Set it and return it to its natural shape in any way you can, then apply on the place the dust of the mill with egg white and soft resin, and put on top a double layer of linen, then put on it a broad piece of wood.
A splint should be as wide as the shoulder or slightly wider, so that if there is a depression under the splint in any of the places on the shoulder, fill this depression with soft resin until the splint lies evenly, then bind it tightly from above with a long bandage.
Tie it firmly and secure the splint so that it does not move from its place, and check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it again and set the splint if it has moved from its place, and let the patient lie on his healthy side, and the shoulder heals in twenty days.
A day or twenty-five days; if this time has passed, the tendon will have healed and you are safe, for it is among the bones whose rupture and tearing are not feared. If a piece of bone protrudes from the bone and was under the skin, it will be broken off. And do as mentioned in the treatment of fractures to relieve the pain, if something like that happens.
Chapter Seven.
It is necessary to set a fracture of the chest.
The chest may fracture in the middle, and this does not usually cause much damage, but its edges are more likely to fracture and protrude. If it fractures in the middle, there will be a tendency to lean downwards, accompanied by severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
There may be bleeding, and the fracture will be evident in the broken bone. The patient should lie on his back with a pillow under his head, then press the ribs together and bring the arms close to the sides, adjusting as needed to make it straight.
And continue until the bone returns to its proper shape, then apply a bandage and splint on top of it. Place a board made of reed or similar material in the sole of the shoe after cutting a slit in it. And then it is skillful in tying it on the broken bone so that it does not slip, and pass the bandage around the torso to the back several times and tighten it firmly, then check the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it again.
And if the need arises to remove it when swelling, pain or inflammation appears in that place, hasten to remove it and cut the bandage and treat what has appeared from it according to its treatment, then replace the bandage if you see fit. And turn him and apply firm pressure until he recovers.
Chapter Eight.
In fractures of the fingers, know that fractures in them occur at rough places located behind the back and their edges facing forward; only the thumb does not show swelling for it is short. Knowing this is not hidden from touch during examination with the fingers.
And set it so that the fracture is aligned with the fingers on the possible face until the shape becomes balanced as needed, then bandage and tighten the broken bone with a splint if necessary, for if the finger fracture is inclined inward, pain will be severe to the patient.
And the fracture is like a fracture that presents with swelling, for the bone swells and also presents difficulty in breathing and coughing, as well as profuse bleeding, and this is difficult to treat, and various methods have been attempted in its treatment.
Some say that the patient's food should be made to produce gas and flatulence, so that the abdomen swells and expands, pushing the fracture outwards. But we reject this, as it might cause inflammation if none has occurred yet; if inflammation is already present, it will increase and intensify it.
Some say that a blistering agent should be applied to the area and then rubbed vigorously, as if measuring it; but there is concern that the agent might draw blood due to the weakness of the area. Others say that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
A pad is placed between the legs until it fills up and makes the bandage even when tied around a cylinder; then the patient is treated with the same diet and medicine as for swelling, for if the patient is in great pain and cannot endure it. The bone was splintered, causing a painful and frightening perforation in the patient, so we must cut into the area and expose the broken shard, then insert beneath it the apparatus that preserves the preceding piece whose description follows, and cut the bone with a saw, and remove it.
Then we will bring the edges of the wound together if it is large, using stitches and treat it with poultices until it heals. If during this process a hot worm appears, hurry to gather the seeds of the rose in fat and place them on the wound, and also treat the patient with what expels the worm from within.
And he should be placed on his unaffected side until he recovers.
The Ninth Chapter.
In the case of a fracture in the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, it is a minor affliction compared to what can happen to them, and worse than that is dislocation. Similarly, the vertebrae of the back; so if this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not. ‌ Look, if you see his hands have become weak, shriveled, and lifeless, and he cannot move them or extend or close them, and if they are cold or pierced by a needle and he does not feel it and there is no pain in them, know that in most cases he will not recover; he is lost.
And if he moves them and feels the bone and membrane, then know that the disease of the bone has left and the patient will heal with treatment. But if the backbone is affected like this, and you want to know whether it also heals or not.
Look at his feet; if you see that they have become numb and experience what happened in the hands, then when he lies on his back, wind and flatulence come out involuntarily. And if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and tries to urinate but cannot, then know that he is dying, so do not bother with treating him. But if none of this happens, the condition is milder. The treatment for what has happened is to apply a poultice to relieve the heat in the inflammation.
To put on the sore, fresh oil of rose or with roasted egg yolk, three times a day until the heat subsides. Then apply one of the strong bandages, wrap it firmly with a bandage, and order him to rest and remain still.
And he should not sleep except on the side that does not have pain until he is healed. If there has been a fracture in the bone due to a fall, it is necessary to split the skin and remove that bone. Then, if the wound is large, join its lips by sewing them together, and treat with bandages soaked in salt until it heals. If another bone of the jaw, which is the lower jaw, is broken, a probe should be inserted from the left hand into the mouth.
And with the other hand, smooth out the broken bone as much as possible until it is straightened. Then place on it a pad and a splint if necessary, and bind it.
If you feel a fracture in it, split the skin and remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
Treatment of a rib fracture.
Rarely do the ribs fracture, and if they do, the fracture is that they split at their ends and crack along their length, and they bend inward, exposing the cause of pain in the place, weakness, and swelling in the leg from which the fracture occurred.
Wrap it in your hand until you reach the break, then examine its shape; if the break is only at the ends, smooth that break according to how you can align it until it resembles its natural shape.
If the break is in the length or if it has gone inside, place the patient on his stomach until you can reduce that break; then, when you have aligned it, apply a bandage to it, and place on it a splint of wood or leather and tighten it firmly.
Do not fear the displacement of the break or the loss of the splint, and fill the spaces between the fingers with what will allow the tightening to take place uniformly. Advise the patient to sleep on his back or on the healthy side, for if he experiences heat.
Stop extending it and reduce it until the heat subsides and place on it what will soothe it, as mentioned earlier; then return to reducing it and tightening it as necessary. If there is a splinter in the bone or something detached from its ends, do not remove it.
Do not touch it, but align it from outside as we mentioned, and leave the tightening until it heals.
Chapter Eleven
On the setting of bone fractures.
If the bone between the elbow and the shoulder breaks, set it in one of two ways. One way is to take a flexible reed of medium hardness and shape it like this:
Then attach two ropes to its ends, suspend it from a high place, and let the patient sit on a chair. Then place his broken arm on the reed so that its end is fastened in the middle of the reed's curve. Afterward, hang something heavy over it.
Or have a servant hold it below, and then the physician will straighten the fracture with his hands until the fracture returns to its proper position. The other method is for the patient to lie on his back, with his hand hanging by a rope from his neck.
Then order two servants: one to hold the area above the fracture with his hands, and the other to hold below it. Each of them should pull in his direction. If you want a stronger pull, tighten a rope below the fracture and another rope above it, and let each servant pull in his respective direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder, place one end of the rope under the elbow and the other under the fracture towards the arm; likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, place the rope in that location and on the elbow itself.
Then, align the fracture gently until it is properly aligned and unified nicely. If there is no sign of infection, secure it; but if there is a sign of infection, leave securing it until the seventh day, then place on it a woolen cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the infection subsides, then secure it. The method of securing is to place a pad on the fracture, then place a new linen bandage over the pad. Then join the arm in the same manner as the hand, and place the hand openly on the shoulder.
Place the linen and the securing apparatus on the arm and hand so that the hand is in the position of a fist, unless something prevents you from doing so. And if nothing changes with the broken bone, then use a splint; but if you fear that something might change, then use a splint.
This is done by placing a splint on the fracture itself, which should be wider and stronger than other splints. There should be the width of one finger between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers from both sides.
And then, apply the second traction on the jaws, which I mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is that your traction should be stronger than the site of the fracture.
And each time the fracture moves, the traction becomes less. If you see the position of the jaws and the traction as we mentioned at the time of your traction for the limb from your hourglass, then act accordingly.
If you fear the hot inflammation, leave the traction and the jaws until the seventh day as we mentioned, then examine the ligature every three days so that no swelling or distention occurs at the site, or food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive traction. All this is treated according to what we have mentioned.
If you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not remove the ligature until many days have passed. The patient should lie on his back with his hand on a pillow, and a moderate pack of wool is placed under the limb.
Check it every night or day to ensure that the shape of the fractured limb does not collapse, and the ligature does not become loose; all this is treated with your effort. And prepare the patient's food according to what we have recommended, that the food should be light at first so that if the bone wishes to heal, it will do so.
It is necessary that his food be bitter, for it is the custom of the toes and feet to become inflamed after forty days; then it is necessary to apply poultices and use the bath, and treat with the plasters that are suitable for this, if the fracture is twisted and swollen.
Do not apply a bandage to him for fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve
In the case of the arm being broken
The arm consists of two bones, one of which is called the ulna and is small, being in front of the elbow; the other is large, lying beneath the small one from below. Sometimes only the large bone breaks, sometimes only the small one, or they may break together.
When the upper small bone breaks, its setting is easy and healing faster; when the lower one breaks, it has a poor fracture and slow healing; and if both bones break together, healing is delayed if the broken bone is the lower small bone.
The physician should make the setting soft and smooth with care until it is aligned; if the large bone is broken, the setting must be firmer; and if both bones are broken, the setting must be very strong.
The hand should be placed when stretching and lengthening on a pillow, with the back of the hand raised higher than all the fingers, and the thumb lower than the rest of the fingers, and the patient sitting cross-legged on himself.
And the pillow should be with him in height so that the patient does not feel discomfort.
Then, an assistant stretches the arm from below either by hand or with a bandage, and another assistant stretches it from above in the same way. The doctor then aligns the bone until he returns it to the best possible shape. If there is a fracture of the bone, it will rupture.
Strive to return every displacement to its place, for if there appears in it a clean fracture and the skin does not allow you to straighten it, cut it open as we mentioned before, because if the break is with a wound, you have provided an exit for it.
Then, take the treatment from there, for if it appears in the first straightening and a hot burn occurs, make an incision with a lancet anointed with rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between the skin and the flesh, and tighten the bandage on it lightly until the pain subsides.
Remove the styptic and place the prepared cautery made from flour mill waste mixed with egg white. Then, carry the cauteries, and let the cautery placed on the fracture be a little wider and stronger. Know that the number of forearm cauteries is usually six.
The fracture was in the shin bone, either alone or in both legs together. Then make your bandage on the site of the fracture stronger and tighter, and whenever you tighten the bandage upwards or downwards, make the bandage slightly looser as mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
The bandage applied on the fracture should be soft and moist, not very hard. The thread used for tightening should be made of moderate thickness cotton between fine and coarse, as we described. Check the limb and the bandage after some days.
If something requiring correction occurs, such as a blister appearing on the limb, it is necessary to soak the limb in clear water until that blister subsides, and then leave the limb unbandaged for one night to rest. Afterwards, bandage again.
If the bandage has become too tight or the bone has moved or nearly so, you must correct it all with your effort. Also look if food does not reach the limb due to excessive tightness, then loosen it slightly and leave it for some days until nourishment reaches it, then bandage it again.
If nothing was applied to the patient from what we mentioned, it should not be uncovered except after twenty days or thereabouts. Then tie the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be moderate, and preserve his strength from disturbing movements, and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days or forty, and sometimes in twenty-eight days; all this depends on the patient's constitution and strength.

And let the splint be made of soft leather, so that it can adapt to the skin with all the movements of the wrist, and bind it as needed. If the break is on the outside, it is necessary to make the splint from above and another splint from below in the wrist, so that the hand remains open and erect.

"Chapter Fourteen.
"On the Forced Setting of Femur Fractures.

Similarly, if the fracture is near the ankle, the band should be close to the ankle so that the pull is downward. "The bone will heal well, so at that time it should bear the load and pressure if there is no hot swelling in the limb. If a swelling occurs, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to treating it.
And as for tightening it, it is necessary to place a broad metal brace on the fracture, twice or thrice its width, with some excess, then bind the leg so that the ankle is at the base of the calf, and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the ankle from below, and the ends of the thread should protrude from both sides.
Then wrap the remaining part of the brace around the leg and the thigh, then place the same splint on the thigh in the location of the fracture, making one splint on the shinbone, then fill the gap between the thigh and the leg with soft bandages to ensure uniform tightness.
Then begin the strong tightening from the middle of the fracture with three or four turns, and as you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, let your tightening be less, softer, and weaker.
Then turn to the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, tie with it what is above it from the splints, then pass the ends of the thread to the bottom until they reach the ankle, also tighten with them the ends of the splints from the other side.
If the ligature slips from its place, then leave the compression on it as long as there is no swelling or inflammation or puffiness and so forth. If any of these occur, hurry to release it and correct whatever happens from that completely as we have mentioned repeatedly.
