Know that the thigh becomes severe after fifty days, or a little more or less, all of this depending on the difference in mixture and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen
On fractures of the ankle joint.
The ankle joint is rarely exposed to fractures, but it is often subjected to dislocation. If it is exposed to a fracture, it will be either broken or separated into its parts, with or without injury.
And stand on that whole with skill, and force her to make level what is separated from its parts by the fingers until they meet and are connected according to the ability of making it level and smooth and perfect, then place the bandage and place on it a circular splint if needed.
And place another bandage on top of that, then treat all the conditions we described for other fractures, such as heat and similar treatments, by addressing each problem with what is suitable for it until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
In the Treatment of Fractures of the Shinbone.
The shinbone has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the tibia, while the other is thin and is called the fibula; both of them can be subject to fractures similar to those that affect the arm bones. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, with one difference: if both bones are fractured, the leg will turn in all directions. If only the thinner bone is fractured, the leg will turn forward. If the thicker bone is fractured and this occurs from below, it is obvious to you.
Use extension and leveling, as well as binding with splints, in the same manner, except that if the fracture of the shinbone is a complex one with many splinters, the bandage should be lighter and softer, and you must exert less effort in its treatment.
In the leg, there is an additional task beyond the arm, which is that when you have straightened the splints and finished all your work on it, take two syringes from the reed used for plastering rooms placed between the planks or made of date palm leaves or similar.
Choose from it that which has little thickness and is not flimsy, and let its length be the length of the leg from the ankle downwards. Then wrap around each one a bandage twice along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
And let it be from the ankle to below the foot, then tie the syringes in three places along their length: on the ends and in the middle. For with this bond, the leg is prevented from leaning to the right or left, and it sets firmly. Wooden pegs can also be used along the length of the leg.
And in it, place something to prevent movement, and this should be done especially in a fracture if there is also a wound. Then examine the leg daily and give it careful attention against infection or swelling or anything else like that. When something like this happens, treat it as appropriate until recovery. The bone of the leg will mend within thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the Fracture of the Bones of the Leg and the Fingers.
As for the heel, it does not commonly suffer a fracture of its bone, but as for the bones of the leg, they may suffer a fracture, and the fingers also rarely suffer a fracture; rather, they usually sustain a dislocation in most cases.
If the bones of the leg suffer a fracture and you see that some of those bones have protruded over others, let the patient place his foot on the ground as if he is walking, then rise and put your foot on what has protruded from those bones, then straighten and align them.
Until they return to their places, then take the splints and bandages from above and place under the sole of the foot a small thin board with two flat sides; then bind the foot tightly with them after unwrapping it from its covering and everything else needed.
After three or four days have passed, release the bandage, for you will find the bones level, whether they were broken or dislocated; then proceed as you see fit. But if one of the fingers is fractured, force and align it as described to you in the section on forcing the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger along its length and slightly wider than it, then place this board under the foot which I have described to you and secure it firmly, for if it is broken from two bones or three or more.
And make on each finger a splint to its length, fitted into a soft bandage, and secure the board under the sole of the foot so that each end of the board protrudes from the underside of the foot for a good fit.
And it is necessary for you to remember not to neglect any of the treatments we have mentioned for all fractures and for each condition as appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
For the fracture of the woman's pelvis, the pubic bone, and the man's penis.
When a woman's pelvis is broken, seat her in a squatting position, then tilt her gently towards her back until she holds herself from behind, and then fill the vagina with cotton so that it becomes like a ball inside her vagina.
Then shake the woman, and her strength will rise a little at a time, for that cotton will come out until it is at the entrance of the vagina like a ball, and the bone fracture will return, then place a cushion on her back so that when she wants to urinate, she removes the cotton with the pad until she urinates.
And it is returned to the way you first entered, then return to its initial position. Do this for seven days or thereabouts, as it will heal. If you wish, take two small tubes of reed and insert them into her mouth, placing the entire tube in her vagina.
Then blow into the tube with force until the tube inflates inside the vagina, and the fracture will return. Then bind it with cotton and leave it for the number of days we described until it heals. As for when a bone is broken in the anus of a man or woman, this method should be used to set it.
This method should be applied as we described for a broken bone in the ankle, and do not neglect the correctness in these strange fractures that rarely occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book well. The fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
And understand the correct way to set and bind it, and as for a man's penis if it is broken, take a small tube of hazel wood and insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of linen over it and let it heal for three days or thereabouts.
Chapter Nineteen
In the setting of fractures with accompanying wounds.
It is necessary for one who has a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large like the thigh or shin, to expedite its setting as soon as possible, for it is assisted by the conditions of setting as we have presented.
If the wound is bleeding, it is necessary to quickly cut around it by drawing a powdered cautery over it, if nothing else is available. Then proceed with setting the fracture that day itself and do not delay it.
If there is no hot swelling, but if there is hot swelling, leave its setting until the ninth day so that the hot swelling subsides. Do not approach it on the third and fourth days of the fever, for he is subject to unpleasant symptoms.
If the broken bone is exposed to the skin, it is necessary to return and straighten it with your hand gently and moderately. If returning and straightening it by hand is not possible for you, then return it using this tool, which is made of iron, its length being seven or eight fingerbreadths long. Show it to the extent of the wound.
Therefore, it is necessary for the doctor to take from it three or four times as much as needed for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be round, so that there is a little oiliness in them, lest they split when pressed during work time.
They should have sharp ends and be thick at the top and very thin from the middle to the bottom, and this is their shape.
And it is called in Greek "πηρος" (piros), meaning a small splint; therefore, its sharp end should be placed at the end of the bone and pushed with it once until when the bone returns to its position somewhat, then saw off the ends of the fracture evenly.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument did not grasp it well, it should be cut until the instrument can get hold of it. And if you cannot return the bone as described with the splint, then cut it according to the sections we mentioned.
Or cut it with one of the saws as best you can, then scrape off what remains in the bone from the skin and thin membranes. If after returning the bone the patient experiences severe pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you are able to return it to its natural place, do so, for the patient will benefit greatly from this. And if the bone is broken, soak a bandage in a solution of black grapes and especially if it is summer, do not apply any ointment or anything containing oil on the wound.
Lest it should putrefy or decay, then use aloe vera after you finish setting the bone and leave the wound exposed, cutting with scissors into small holes to match the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to close the wound with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians do this and cause their patients either death or a serious illness such as gangrene or tetanus. Therefore, suture the wound gently and in contrast to the closure of other fractures.
If the wound is small or if it is a large wound and you fear some of the unpleasant consequences that we have described, and you find the pain in the place to be disturbing, then it is not proper to apply aloe vera on it. Instead, make bandages from firm cloth in the place of aloe vera and close it with them.
And if after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to generate pus, remove the cloth soaked in the solution and then use honeycomb and cotton that we are accustomed to using for healing wounds such as honeycomb and similar, and it is appropriate for you to moisten the bandage.
The wound should be examined every evening and morning until it heals and clears, and the limb should be set in a position that allows the pus to drain easily downwards. If many days have passed for the wound and it has not closed or the pus has not stopped flowing from it, know that there are small bone fragments present.
It is necessary to probe the wound with a hook; if any of those fragments are free, remove them and extract them. If they are not free and the limb is swollen and painful, crush them, cut them off, and remove them by every means possible.
If the wound develops gangrene, a sore, or any other form of corruption and foulness, it is necessary to treat each of these conditions with the appropriate remedy that has been mentioned in its relevant chapter.
And what you should be aware of, and keep in mind, is that if a large bone breaks, such as the femur or the humerus, and similar major limbs, do not attempt to extract it or remove it. Because often death results from attempting to remove it, leave it until it is absorbed, which may happen after twenty or thirty days.
When treating a wound, if you see a place for treatment, then treat it; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty
In the treatment of adhesions that occur as a result of some fractures.
This adhesion often occurs after the fracture, especially if it is near the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb, sometimes preventing it from performing its natural function.
If the adhesion is recent, use medicines that contract, such as patience, milk, honey, myrrh, aloes and similar, taking some or all of them and mixing with a contracting drink, egg white or vinegar.
And place them on the adhesion with irritation and bind them firmly, leaving the bandage for several days, then remove it and repeat another until the adhesion disappears, if God wills. Or bind a firm lead sheet to it.
For lead has the property of removing everything that occurs in the limbs, so if the adhesion becomes hard and the necessity to remove it increases, then cut into it from above and excise or cauterize the resulting lump until it disappears and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of fractures, if it is reduced and the limb remains thin afterwards compared to its original shape.
If a bone fracture is reduced and the limb remains thin and weak afterwards, this can be due to many reasons. One reason is the excessive unraveling and tying of bandages in an incorrect manner, or the excessive tightening of bandages to the point where food cannot reach the limb, or excessive movement in the wrong time, or the scarcity of blood and its weakness in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and improve his constitution until blood increases in him, and to use the bath and introduce joy and happiness into him, then to place a plaster on the limb so that it draws abundant or continuous food to it, or to continually rinse it with cold water. Until the nutrition flows into it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of broken bones, if they are reduced but do not perform their function as needed.
When a limb is exposed due to the absence of a bone or because of a broken bone, or due to swelling and inflammation, it appears in this way. However, the limb has not deviated from its natural function, so it is not appropriate to accept the opinion of those who claim that a limb can be broken from its base. For many ignorant doctors and quacks have done this in our country.
This act is highly reprehensible and leads to great deception, easing the disease. But if the swelling and inflammation are severe, it is necessary to apply water in which mallow leaves such as hollyhock and its root, and king's-spear have been boiled, and similar things.
It is compressed with the soft mucilage of the well-made diachylon or the root of hollyhock is taken and mixed with chicken fat and castor oil and it is compressed with it, or the leaves of unripe figs are taken and mixed with pigeon dung and similar drugs that break up tumors.
And the persistent thin silk that is available in the hands is used to dissolve swelling, and the movement of the limb in all directions during all times is utilized. If the swelling has advanced and intensified and hardened, and necessity forces its treatment with iron.
It is necessary to open the top, separate the bone connection, and cut what remains of the tumor or bone with thin sections, using care and effort in doing so. Then treat the wound with what was mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Three and Twenty.
A fracture is a dislocation of the joint from its place, which hinders movement and distorts the limb's shape, causing the patient severe pain. Therefore, as soon as someone suffers a fracture, it is necessary to hurry from that moment until he is treated.
One should not delay treating it, for if delayed, the location will swell and make returning the fracture difficult. Therefore, it should not be delayed or moved or extended when it swells, as it often causes the patient discomfort and severe pain.
But if this happens, it is necessary to hurry to place the patient in a sitting position and leave him until the swelling subsides somewhat. Then dip the limb in hot water and oil, and return it with care, treating each part as mentioned for its respective area.
The sections on fractures have also been arranged according to the location of the break from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four
In the treatment of lower jaw fracture.
If the fracture is incomplete, it rarely heals completely; its removal will result in one of two outcomes: either the pieces will be dislodged slightly and heal with minimal scarring, or they will be removed entirely until healing occurs. Towards the chest, so that the patient's saliva flows and he cannot hold it back, nor can he speak clearly due to his tongue slipping; but if its removal is easy, it usually returns to its place more easily.
However, if the removal is complete, it is necessary to hasten its return with speed and not delay it; this means that an assistant holds the patient's head while the physician inserts his index finger into the base of the fracture within the mouth if the fracture is on one side.
Or he may insert both of his index fingers if the fracture is from both sides, and with the rest of his fingers from outside, he aligns it and instructs the patient to heal his fracture and let it go in both directions, while the physician aligns the fracture and pushes it until it returns to its place.
It is difficult to return it, especially if all the parts are together. Therefore, use water and oil with the paste until they become easier to remove. Do not delay their removal, as we said. Then tie it with a bandage made of soft fabric, so that the patient sleeps on his back and his head is supported between two pillows to prevent him from moving to the right or left or exerting himself in chewing; rather, make his food very soft.
Until the pain disappears and the jaw is healed, let him eat whatever he desires, using a bandage for that purpose, and not open his mouth when eating, drinking, or speaking until the jaw is fully healed and cured.
If it is difficult to return the jaw, especially if it dislocates at any time and does not return to its place, then often swelling and persistent inflammation occur, and sometimes the patient's belly protrudes, or the testicles become hot with heat; if you see this, know that it has deteriorated.

Chapter Twenty-Six
"On the reduction of a fractured cheekbone.

As for those with much flesh, it is contrary to that; meaning it exits slowly and enters slowly. Sometimes a blow or fall may bruise the abdomen and cause intense heat, leading one to believe that it has leaked. It is necessary to examine this until you are certain of its soundness.

He should not move his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be made light so that the limb may gain strength, which will hasten its recovery. Then, after seven or five days, he should exercise it; if it holds fast and does not dislocate again, it is cured.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to excessive moisture or another cause, it is necessary to use a splint with three strips of cloth as described in the chapter on splints. If this is all done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint does not hold,
"The tendon and sinew were tightened several times, but it did not set; it fell and relaxed, and you could not raise it again. So know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been cut or stretched or relaxed, and then know that the joint will never set again in its place.
"As for the break that occurs near the chest and the shoulder blade to the back, it is set by pressing and stretching with the hands until it returns, and all the tightening and treatment are used in it until it heals. If after healing a swelling appears in the limb and movement becomes slower.
"The patient should use the bath frequently until that swelling softens and returns to its original state.
"Chapter Twenty-Seven.
"On the Treatment of Fractures of the Ribs.
"The rib fracture is slow to heal and also returns slowly, as it can fracture in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its fracture does not go unnoticed because it is evident under sight and touch in any form of fracture.
"If the broken rib is close to a healthy one, this will be evident to you by the swelling of the fracture, and it will not allow the arm to bend or touch its shoulder. It is necessary to hasten and return the fracture as soon as possible before pus forms.
It is because if a hot worm afflicts it, returning it is difficult, and perhaps the ulcer does not heal, especially if the ulcer is in the back. For it is the weakest of all types of ulcers, and the most painful, and death often comes with it.
If it can be returned, it is necessary for an assistant to hold the patient's hand with both of his hands and straight arms, while the physician holds from above the joint and below it. He then pushes the dislocation with all his fingers or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
As for if the dislocation is in front, it may return by bending the hand once so that the palm strikes against the adjacent bone. If the dislocation does not return, then use a strong force with two assistants holding the arm.
The patient should also be held by two assistants so that he does not move when the force is applied. Then, the arm is rotated in all directions after wrapping a cloth or wide bandage around his hand. If the physician touches the dislocation with his hand, he should smear it with oil to facilitate its easy displacement.
Then, the dislocated part is pushed back with great force until it returns to its place. After it returns, a bandage containing compressive and drying agents along with egg white should be applied, pressed firmly, and the arm suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
Then, if the dislocated part has stabilized in its place, remove the bandage and leave it; however, if you see that the dislocation is not firm, then renew the bandage and leave it for a few more days until it becomes firm, after which you can remove it. If swelling occurs after the dislocated part returns and is removed.
If there is difficulty in movement, use warm baths and gentle massage until it becomes supple, or place a piece of fatty mutton on the dislocation, tie it securely, and leave it for a day and night, then remove it and enter the bath. If swelling occurs, drain the swelling with a moderate bleeding.
Then, apply the fatty piece on him again and again, up to three times with each entry into the bath until it becomes supple. If you wish, you can also place a moist cow dung mixed with oil on him and press it firmly; do this several times, for it will become supple and return to its original state.
The Twenty-Eighth Chapter
On the Treatment of a Fractured Bone
The bone of the hand often breaks, and its fracture is easier to reduce than the rest of the joints, except that one must hurry to reduce the fracture within an hour after it breaks before heat or inflammation develops in the place.
Reduce the fracture by placing the patient's bone on a board, and have an assistant hold his hand while the physician places his palm on the joint's tip and pushes it until it returns to its place. However, he must check if the fracture has gone into the depth of the hand.
Let the patient place the back of his hand on the board during stretching and reduction, and if the fracture is prominent to the back of the hand, let him place the palm of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same joint tip. If it returns from there, and not, then tighten it with a bandage to alleviate pain.
And leave it, for it cannot be repeated, and it is not possible to reduce it again after days have passed except that the joint remains swollen, and it does not harm the patient except if the hand is paralyzed and unable to grasp anything.
When you learn that the nerve has been cut or crushed, there is no hope for it except to splint with a wooden stick; sometimes it helps and sometimes it does not help at all. So if the sinew is restored, put on it the dressing we described, then splint and leave for five days.
Then, it is unbound and the hand is exercised; if its movement is impeded or any stiffness appears in it, let it be bathed with hot water and oil repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the treatment of fractured fingers.
If a finger is fractured in any direction, when it is fractured from the finger towards the back of the palm or its inner side, stretch the finger and push the fracture with your thumb until it returns, then bind the tip of the finger and suspend it towards the direction to which it was fractured.
Leave it for two days, then release and stretch it until it stands upright by itself that day; if it is at night, bind it as described above, but unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, binding it again at night. Continue doing this for days until it stiffens.
If it sinks to the depth of the hand, bind it in a similar manner towards the same direction and treat it as you did the first time until it heals. Do the same if it sinks to any other part.
Chapter Thirty
In treating a hernia in the back
If a hernia occurs in the back or neck and it is complete, or if many hernias have fallen away from their places, there is no treatment for it, because death quickly approaches the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's abdomen protrudes without control, and often his leg or arm, or one of them, becomes numb. However, if only one hernia falls away from its place, it usually returns.
It can fall in four directions: if it falls to the back, it is called a 'hernia'; as for its treatment, there is no treatment for it and no chance of recovery if it occurred from pregnancy. However, if it happened due to a fall or blow, etc.,
As for the treatment of hernia, it has been mentioned in various ways by many with long speeches that are not of much benefit. Some have summarized what is necessary from this. I will provide a brief account of what was conveyed to me, explaining and illustrating it differently than what they have shown and explained.
"I say that the swelling which occurs in front of the chest has no cure and cannot be avoided, as well as those on both sides. The only treatment is for that which occurs in the back, as I will describe: the patient should lie face down.
"Place a trestle near the wall and spread underneath it a wet cloth so that his chest is not harmed. Then place a vertical stick, stuck in a hole in the ground next to the head of the patient, and another near his feet on the opposite side of the trestle.
"And let one servant hold the stick and make it unstable in the hole, and another servant hold the other stick in the same way. Then they turn him onto his chest and tie his limbs with soft and strong cords, extending the ends of the cords to the stick near his head and tying them there.
"Then they bind his wrists and ankles with other cords, and near his elbows. Then all the cords are gathered together and tied to the other stick near his feet. Then each servant pulls on the cord toward the stick until both sticks remain in their fixed positions.
The only problem is that they are not fixed as we mentioned, and the physician places his hand on the string with force until it returns or he places a board on it, then stands on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board whose length is about three spans.
And a hole is dug in the wall which we mentioned should be near the patient, into which one end of the board is inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the hump and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until the string becomes taut.
And it returns to its place. If you wish, you can make it with a rope that is wound around the hand, as you dig two pieces of wood in the ground at the patient's head, each being an arm's length long, and likewise at his feet.
And there should be a space of one finger's width between each piece of wood. A hole is made in each piece of wood through which the rope passes, and all the pieces of wood are firmly fixed in the ground so that they do not move. Then insert a turned spindle—this is the rope that is wound around it—through the two holes in the pieces of wood.
In its side, make a hole in which you insert an arrow of the length that can be bent, and in the other two wooden pieces likewise; then tighten the ligature that you have tied on the chest of the patient at the end near the head, and the ligatures that you have tied on his leg at the end near the foot.
Then, a servant stands by each ligature, unraveling with his hand the reel which bends the ligature; and the doctor adjusts the board as we have mentioned. And this is the image of the ligatures, the reels, and the patient.
Then, after the swelling has subsided and the place is level, it is necessary to place a dry poultice with egg white, then wool, and above the poultice place a board whose width is three fingers or similar, and its length according to the size of the board.
And on some of the correct herbs, then tie with the ligature that is necessary; and let the patient use light food until he recovers. If there remains some swelling in the place at the end of the recovery, it is necessary to use drugs that cause sweating and softening along with using the board that we have described for a long time.
And for that, a leaden plate is used; and if there is another swelling on the back of the chest, it may be believed to be a broken bone, which has not come out, so do not expose it to this treatment, lest death occur.
Chapter Thirty-One
In the Treatment of Fractured Skulls
Know that the skull joint and the jaw joint are only dislocated, and nothing is applied to them like the slightest wasting or languor which is applied to the rest of the joints. The skull joint may be dislocated in four ways.
And that is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward; and most often it dislocates inward, while rarely does it dislocate forward or backward. The sign of its dislocation inward is that if you measure the sound leg of the patient with a ruler, it will be longer.
And the thigh will be shorter than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to lift his leg when standing; and the place which is near the knee will appear sunken and hollow because the end of the femur has moved there.
The sign of one whose jaw protrudes to the front is that his leg straightens completely, except he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and even if he tries to walk, he cannot do so forward.
His urine will be retained, and his back will become humped. When he walks, his gait will be on his heels. The sign of one whose jaw protrudes to the rear is that they cannot straighten their knee or bend it before the ankle bends. Their leg will also be shorter than the other.
The spine will be curved, and the head of the thigh bone at the knee joint will be prominent. As for reversing this type of jaw, it is to gaze if the protrusion is old and has persisted with its owner without trying to reverse it and remains in that state, there is no cure for it.
It is not advisable for it to be exposed. As for the one whose protrusion is recent, and regardless of which of the four types of protrusion he has, he should hasten to loosen the joint and extend it inward and outward, moving it gently and softly, perhaps.
He returned, not needing any other treatment. If he does not return in this manner, it is necessary to prepare a strong servant who supports his leg from below, either with his hands or with bands tied around the leg above the ankle, and another servant supporting him from above.
He should insert his hand under the patient's hip, then tighten soft bands around the root of the thigh, and another servant should hold onto the end of the bands from the third side. The support can be either from in front, from the side of the navel to the side of the back, or from behind to the side of the spine.
All of them should support him together in one go until the patient is raised off the ground and remains suspended, for this type of support is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns as we said, then each type requires its specific treatment.
If the dislocation is inward, it is necessary for the patient to lie on his sound side, and then bands are placed around the root of the thigh between the top of the thigh and the place beneath the navel.
Then the rope is pulled from above, from the side of the navel to the highest part of the body to the side of the back. Another strong assistant then supports with his arms and embraces the hot place on the dislocated thigh, and pulls strongly outward, for it returns to its place.
This type is the easiest of all treatments for this limb, and if it is difficult for you to enter with this type of treatment, then you should tie the patient's feet together with a strong ligament on the heels and ankles.
And after each one, the length is four fingers, and the affected leg will be extended more than the other by two fingers. Then the patient hangs from a beam in the house, with his distance from the ground being two arms' lengths.
Then you order a strong young man to embrace the head of the thigh and another youth to hang onto the patient, and that other youth pushes strongly against the embracing youth on the thigh so that the joint returns quickly to its place, but if it is a dislocation outwards.
The patient should then lie on the plank as described for the patient with a hernia, and the ligature is tightened around his affected leg and chest. Then one board is placed at his feet and another at his head, and an additional board is inserted firmly in the middle of the plank.
It is wrapped with wet bandage so that the patient will not be harmed, and there will be a board between his thighs to prevent it from slipping down during treatment. Then each assistant pulls in his direction, and the physician straightens the bone with his hands. If there is a response to correction.
If not, place the board on it and press as we mentioned in the dislocation, except that the patient should be lying on his healthy side. And if the dislocation is forward, it is necessary to stretch the patient's leg once while he is in this position under the carpenter.
The physician places his right hand on the patient's ankle, then presses it with the other hand while extending the pressure down to the foot, and if the dislocation is backward, it is not necessary for the patient to stretch downward.
It should be raised from the ground, but rather placed on something solid, as his clothes and bandages should also be turned outward according to what we mentioned about his position under the carpenter while he is on his back with the bindings tightened.
On what we said about the anus, it is also necessary to use a bandage on the site from which the hernia protrudes. If the hernia returns to its place as it should and its return does not hide from you, then it is that the patient's leg extends.
If you see them both level and the patient is able to contract and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the part has returned to its proper place. At that time, bind the thighs together, place a bandage, and tighten it with a cord so that the hernia does not move in any direction.
The patient must rest for three or four days, then remove the bandage and the splint, and bind the leg with the other. If you see them both equal in size, know that the rupture has healed, and release the patient to walk. But if you see any swelling in it.
Then return and bandage it, tightening it as you did the first time, leaving it for another three days. After that, remove it, and he should walk on them gradually until he feels strong again, God willing.
Chapter Two and Thirty-Two
In the treatment of hip dislocation.
The thigh swells in three directions: it swells inward, outward, and downward, meaning to the back, and does not swell toward the front of the foot, and the sign of its fracture is that the patient is ordered to pull his shin to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, know that the thigh is broken. All signs of its fracture are that the patient sits with his leg extended if he has the strength to do so, and a servant sits behind him holding his middle and gently drawing it back, then you sit on his thigh.
And you adhere your back to his face and place his leg between yours, then you hold his thigh with both hands and intertwine them on his thigh, then you pull its side with your hand with force, and another servant extends his leg until the thigh returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the shin adheres to the thigh with ease, not forcefully. Then you bind it and adhere the shin to the thigh, then tie them all together with three or four splints for three days or four, then release them. And he should only walk a little each day until it gains strength.
If it is difficult for you to return (it) with what we have described, then use the strong rope with the knots that were previously described for treating ulcers until (it) returns.
The Thirty-Third Section.
In the treatment of fractures of the ankle.
The ankle may swell slightly or completely dislocate, and the dislocation can be either inward or outward. A sign of its dislocation is that you will see the ankle prominently swollen towards the direction it has dislocated to.
As for the treatment of its swelling, it is easy to return it by stretching with hands and straightening until it returns. However, if it dislocates completely, it is necessary that the patient sits with a strong assistant holding from behind the back in the middle.
Then, hold his ankle with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below the ankle in the Achilles tendon area. Then pull his foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand twice, then straighten it with your left hand and push the heel of the foot towards the shin with your right hand without force.
Do this twice as described, then in the third time push the heel towards the shin while you pull on the Achilles tendon. If it returns in one or two tries this way and you see the foot is level, then stop. If not, repeat the process, for it will return.
If you cannot return it as described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and dig a hole in the earth that is very secure so that it falls between his thighs. Then place a cloth over him to prevent injury to the patient, and then bind his thigh.
Then another assistant supports the patient's feet, either with his hands or with a band that he ties around the patient's neck. Each assistant then supports in the opposite direction of his partner, and a wooden wedge is placed between the patient's thighs to prevent his body from being drawn down during the support, then the physician levels the fracture with his hand.
And another assistant holds the healthy leg down, for the fracture returns quickly. If the fracture returns and its correct return is evident to you, carry the splint and the bandage, and strengthen it with strong bands, and tie the foot to the lower end of the bandage. Be cautious of the nerve that is on top of the heel at the back.
To avoid the bandage being too tight and hurting him, then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, strengthen it and release it on the third or fourth day, and prevent the patient from walking for forty days.
If the wound has been walked on before this period, it cannot be guaranteed that the bone will not fracture and corrupt, and after that, treatment will not be accepted. Therefore, if heat is applied to it, it should be treated with what we have described in another place for treatment and bandaging until it subsides.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of fractures of the toes.
It is necessary to straighten what has been broken from it with a short length without violence, and this is not difficult but easy. If the fracture is in some of the bones of the back of the foot, the patient should place his foot on a level spot on the ground.
Or on a board while standing as if walking, then you should place your foot on top of those joints and press with your foot firmly until it returns and you see that it is straightened and nothing sticks out in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that takes the whole foot, having two ends. Then bind it with a firm and secure bandage for three days, then unwrap it and prevent him from walking for many days until it heals and ensures recovery, God willing.
The Fifth Chapter
In the types of fracture that occurs with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both together.
When something like this happens and its treatment is delayed, it often results in death.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for someone other than one who is proficient in this skill, with extensive experience, precise, compassionate, and well-composed to undertake the treatment of such cases. Also, he should use in the beginning only those medicines that soothe hot swellings, and then hand over the patient.
O Lord, let not that which I hoped for safety from the disease be harmed by the slightness of the illness and some hope appeared to you in it, so return it quickly from your hands in the first matter before the hot inflammation occurs.
So if the limb returns to its original position, use the remedy that soothes hot swellings and treat the wound with what is suitable for it from dry dressings. But if the fracture is accompanied by a splinter or shard, remove it immediately. And in that, follow what we mentioned regarding simple diseases before, in their respective places.
Your effort and resolve will prevent you from entering the path of illusions, following my advice to you. Thus, you will remain on your way and submit to your destiny, God willing.
